    Appendix C Solutions to Selected Exercises

    This is an introduction, where you might explain that this division of the back matter contains various hints, answers, solutions of inline exercises, divisional exercises, project-like blocks, worksheet exercises, and/or reading questions. See the source to see just how this solutions division was built.

    1 Sistem Persamaan
    1.1 Apa yang dapat kita harapkan
    1.1.1 Beberapa contoh sederhana

    Activity 1.1.1.

    1.1.1.a
    Answer.
    Hanya ada satu titik, yaitu titik \((2,3)\text{,}\) yang memenuhi kedua persamaan tersebut.
    1.1.1.b
    Answer.
    Tidak ada titik yang memenuhi kedua persamaan tersebut.
    1.1.1.c
    Answer.
    Terdapat tak hingga banyaknya titik.
    1.1.1.d
    Answer.
    Tidak ada titik yang memenuhi ketiga persamaan tersebut.

    Activity 1.1.2.

    Answer.
    1. Ya.
    2. Tidak.
    3. Ya.
    4. Kita mengharapkan tidak ada solusi.
    5. Kita mengharapkan tidak ada solusi.
    6. Kita mengharapkan terdapat tak hingga banyak solusi.

    2 Vectors, matrices, and linear combinations
    2.1 Vektor dan kombinasi linear
    2.1.2 Kombinasi linear

    Activity 2.1.2.

    Answer.
    1. Kombinasi linearnya terletak pada garis yang didefinisikan oleh \(\vvec\text{.}\)
    2. \(\twovec{0}{-3}\text{.}\)
    3. Mereka terletak pada garis melalui \(\wvec\) sejajar dengan \(\vvec\text{.}\)
    4. Ya, dengan bobot \(c = d = 0\text{.}\)
    5. Ya, dengan bobot \(c = 2\) dan \(d = -1\text{.}\)
    6. Hal ini dapat dilakukan dengan menyusun sistem linear yang sesuai untuk bobot-bobot tersebut.
    7. Tidak, setiap vektor dua dimensi dapat dinyatakan sebagai kombinasi linear dari \(\vvec\) dan \(\wvec\text{.}\)

    Activity 2.1.3. Kombinasi linear dan sistem linear.

    Answer.
    1. Vektor \(\bvec\) tidak dapat dinyatakan sebagai kombinasi linear dari \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) dan \(\vvec_3\text{.}\)
    2. Vektor-vektor yang bersesuaian adalah:
      \begin{equation*} \vvec_1=\threevec{3}{1}{-1}, \vvec_2=\threevec{2}{0}{-1}, \vvec_3=\threevec{-1}{2}{3}, \bvec=\threevec{4}{0}{1}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    3. Ya, \(\bvec\) dapat dinyatakan sebagai kombinasi linear dari \(\vvec_1, \vvec_2, \vvec_3\) dalam tak hingga banyak cara.
    4. Tidak.
    5. Ya, \(\bvec\) dapat dinyatakan sebagai kombinasi linear dari \(\vvec_1, \vvec_2, \vvec_3\) dalam tepat satu cara.
    6. Setiap vektor \(\bvec\) dapat dinyatakan sebagai kombinasi linear dari \(\vvec_1, \vvec_2, \vvec_3\) dalam tepat satu cara.

    2.1.4 Exercises

    2.1.4.1.

    Answer.
    3. This forms the line passing through \(2\vvec\) parallel to \(\wvec\text{.}\)
    4. There are many possibilities. One is \(\vvec=\twovec{0}{-2}\) and \(\wvec=\twovec{1}{3}\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    3. This forms the line passing through \(2\vvec\) parallel to \(\wvec\text{.}\)
    4. There are many possibilities. One is \(\vvec=\twovec{0}{-2}\) and \(\wvec=\twovec{1}{3}\text{.}\)

    2.1.4.2.

    Answer.
    1. We have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{lll} p = -\vvec, \amp q = 3\wvec, \amp r=2\vvec+3\wvec, \\ s = 2\vvec-3\wvec, \amp t=-3\vvec-2\wvec, \amp u=-2\vvec+0.5\wvec, \\ v = 3.5\vvec+1.5\wvec\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    2. This is the line passing through \(-2\vvec\) parallel to \(\wvec\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{lll} p = -\vvec, \amp q = 3\wvec, \amp r=2\vvec+3\wvec, \\ s = 2\vvec-3\wvec, \amp t=-3\vvec-2\wvec, \amp u=-2\vvec+0.5\wvec, \\ v = 3.5\vvec+1.5\wvec\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    2. This is the line passing through \(-2\vvec\) parallel to \(\wvec\text{.}\)

    2.1.4.3.

    Answer.
    1. The linear combination
      \begin{equation*} 2\vvec_1-3\vvec_2+\vvec_3 = \twovec{5}{-1}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    2. \(c_1 = \frac23 c_3\) and \(c_2 = -\frac23 c_3\text{.}\)
    3. \(c_1=c_2=0\text{.}\)
    4. \(\vvec_3=-\frac23\vvec_1 + \frac23\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The linear combination
      \begin{equation*} 2\vvec_1-3\vvec_2+\vvec_3 = \twovec{5}{-1}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    2. The appropriate linear system corresponds to the augmented matrix
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 2 \amp -1 \amp -2 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp -\frac{2}{3} \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp \frac{2}{3} \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      This shows we obtain \({\mathbf 0}\) when the weights are chosen so that
      \begin{equation*} \begin{alignedat}{2} c_1 \amp {}={} \frac23 c_3 \\ c_2 \amp {}={} -\frac23 c_3\text{.} \\ \end{alignedat} \end{equation*}
    3. In this case, we want \(c_3=0\text{,}\) which means that \(c_1=c_2=0\text{.}\)
    4. We see that \(\vvec_3=-\frac23\vvec_1 + \frac23\vvec_2\text{.}\)

    2.1.4.4.

    Answer.
    1. \(\threevec{120}{105}{1.0}\text{.}\)
    2. The totals consumed are expressed by the vector
      \begin{equation*} c\threevec{111}{140}{1.2} + d\threevec{120}{105}{1.0}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    3. You had two servings of Frosted Flakes and one serving of Cocoa Puffs.
    4. Your sister must have eaten something else.
    Solution.
    1. \(\threevec{120}{105}{1.0}\text{.}\)
    2. The totals consumed are expressed by the vector
      \begin{equation*} c\threevec{111}{140}{1.2} + d\threevec{120}{105}{1.0}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    3. We ask to write the vector \(\threevec{342}{385}{3.4}\) as a linear combination of the two cereal vectors. This leads to the augmented matrix
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 111 \amp 120 \amp 342 \\ 140 \amp 105 \amp 385 \\ 1.2 \amp 1.0 \amp 3.4 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      This means that you had two servings of Frosted Flakes and one serving of Cocoa Puffs.
    4. Now the augmented matrix is
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 111 \amp 120 \amp 250 \\ 140 \amp 105 \amp 200 \\ 1.2 \amp 1.0 \amp 4.0 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{,} \end{equation*}
      which represents an inconsistent system. This means that your sister must have eaten something else.

    2.1.4.5.

    Answer.
    1. The vector \(\bvec\) may be expressed as a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_3\) provided that the weights are related by \(c_1=5-2c_3\) and \(c_2=2-2c_3\text{.}\)
    2. The vector \(\bvec\) cannot be written as a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_3\text{.}\)
    3. \(\vvec_3=2\vvec_1+2\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    4. \(a\vvec_1+b\vvec_2+c\vvec_3= (a+2c)\vvec_1+(b+2c)\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We have the system corresponding to the augmented matrix
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 2 \amp 0 \amp 4 \amp 10 \\ -1 \amp 3 \amp 4 \amp 1 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp -2 \amp -8 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp 2 \amp 5 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 2 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      from which we conclude that \(\bvec\) may be expressed as a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_3\) provided that the weights are related by \(c_1=5-2c_3\) and \(c_2=2-2c_3\text{.}\)
    2. Here we have
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 2 \amp 0 \amp 4 \amp 3 \\ -1 \amp 3 \amp 4 \amp 7 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp -2 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp 2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right]\text{,} \end{equation*}
      which represents an inconsistent system and shows that \(\bvec\) cannot be written as a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_3\text{.}\)
    3. The augmented matrix is
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 2 \amp 0 \amp 4 \\ -1 \amp 3 \amp 4 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp -2 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{,} \end{equation*}
      which shows that \(\vvec_3=2\vvec_1+2\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    4. \(a\vvec_1+b\vvec_2+c\vvec_3=a\vvec_1+b\vvec_2 + c(2\vvec_1+2\vvec_2) = (a+2c)\vvec_1+(b+2c)\vvec_2\text{.}\)

    2.1.4.6.

    Answer.
    \(k=10\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    We form the augmented matrix and find a triangular matrix that is row equivalent:
    \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 3 \amp 1 \amp k \\ -1 \amp 1 \amp -2 \\ 1 \amp 2 \amp 5 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} -1 \amp 1 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp k-10 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
    This represents a consistent system when \(k=10\text{.}\)

    2.1.4.7.

    Answer.
    1. True
    2. True
    3. True
    4. False
    Solution.
    1. True, because we can choose the weights \(c=2\) and \(d=0\text{.}\)
    2. True, because the augmented matrix \(\left[\begin{array}{rrrr|r} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \amp \ldots \amp \vvec_n \amp \bvec \end{array}\right]\) can never have a pivot position in the rightmost column.
    3. True, because the augmented matrix \(\left[\begin{array}{rrrr|r} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \amp \ldots \amp \vvec_n \amp \bvec \end{array}\right]\) can never have a pivot position in the rightmost column and the corresponding linear system cannot have a free variable.
    4. False, because it is possible to choose a vector \(\bvec\) such that the augmented matrix \(\left[\begin{array}{rr|r} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \amp \bvec \end{array}\right]\) has a pivot in the rightmost column.

    2.1.4.8.

    Answer.
    1. \((1,-4) = \{2,-3\}\text{.}\)
    2. \(\{7/3, -8/3\} = (2,-3)\text{.}\)
    3. \begin{equation*} \begin{alignedat}{2} x \amp {}={} \amp 2c+d \\ y \amp {}={} \amp c+2d\text{.} \\ \end{alignedat} \end{equation*}
    4. Solve the linear system corresponding to the augmented matrix
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 2 \amp 1 \amp x \\ 1 \amp 2 \amp y \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
    Solution.
    1. We have
      \begin{equation*} \twovec{x}{y} = 2\vvec_1 -3\vvec_2 = \twovec{1}{-4}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    2. We have \(\twovec{2}{-3} = c\vvec_1 + d\vvec_2 \text{.}\) Solving this equation, we find \(c=7/3\) and \(d=-8/3\text{,}\) which means that \((c,d) = \{7/3, -8/3\}\text{.}\)
    3. As in the first part of this problem, we write
      \begin{equation*} \begin{alignedat}{2} x \amp {}={} \amp 2c+d \\ y \amp {}={} \amp c+2d\text{.} \\ \end{alignedat} \end{equation*}
    4. We want to solve \(\twovec{x}{y} = c\vvec_1 + d\vvec_2\) by constructing the augmented matrix and finding its reduced row echelon form.
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 2 \amp 1 \amp x \\ 1 \amp 2 \amp y \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}

    2.2.4 Matrix-matrix products

    Activity 2.2.5.

    Answer.
    1. The product \(AB\) exists because the number of columns of \(A\) equals the number of rows of \(B\text{.}\) The dimensions of \(AB\) are \(2\times 2\text{.}\)
    2. We have \(AB = \left[\begin{array}{rr} 2 \amp 4 \\ -3 \amp 9 \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. Define
      A = matrix(2, 3, [1, 3, 2, -3, 4, -1])
B = matrix(3, 2, [3, 0, 1, 2, -2, -1])
A*B
    4. It is not generally true that \(AB=BA\text{.}\)
    5. We find that \(A(B+C)=AB + AC\text{.}\)
    6. We find that \(A(BC) = (AB)C\text{.}\)
    7. It is not generally true that \(B=C\) if \(AB=AC\text{.}\)
    8. It is not generally true that \(A=0\) or \(B=0\) if \(AB=0\text{.}\)

    5. The solution space will form a line in \(\real^4\text{,}\) as in part b.

      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrrr|r} 1 \amp 2 \amp -4 \amp -4 \amp -1 \\ 2 \amp 3 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 15 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 4 \amp 6 \amp 17 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 2 \amp 9 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp -1 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \amp 2 \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}

    5. The coefficient matrix \(\left[\begin{array}{r|r} A \amp \bvec \end{array}\right]\) will have exactly one column without a pivot position. Therefore, the solution space will form a line in \(\real^4\text{,}\) as in part b.

    2.2.6.7.

    Answer.
    1. To scale the third row by \(-3\text{,}\) we would use the matrix \(S= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp -3 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. To interchange the first and third rows, we would use \(P= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. To multiply the first row by 3 and add to the third row, we would use \(L_2= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 3 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. \(L= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ -1 \amp 2 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We compute that
      \begin{equation*} SA = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 7 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 2 \amp -1 \\ 2 \amp 0 \amp 2 \\ -3 \amp 2 \amp 3 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 2 \amp -1 \\ 14 \amp 0 \amp 14 \\ -3 \amp 2 \amp 3 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      To scale the third row by \(-3\text{,}\) we would use the matrix \(S= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp -3 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. We compute that
      \begin{equation*} PA = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 2 \amp -1 \\ 2 \amp 0 \amp 2 \\ -3 \amp 2 \amp 3 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 2 \amp 0 \amp 2 \\ 1 \amp 2 \amp -1 \\ -3 \amp 2 \amp 3 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      To interchange the first and third rows, we would use \(P= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. We compute that
      \begin{equation*} L_1A = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 2 \amp -1 \\ 2 \amp 0 \amp 2 \\ -3 \amp 2 \amp 3 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 2 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp -4 \amp 4 \\ -3 \amp 2 \amp 3 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      To multiply the first row by 3 and add to the third row, we would use \(L_2= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 3 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. Keeping track of the operations we perform in Gaussian elimination, we define
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{cc} L_1= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right], \amp L_2= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 3 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right], \\ L_3 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 2 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
      Then \(L_3L_2L_1A = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 2 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp -4 \amp 4 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 8 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\) So \(L=L_3L_2L_1 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ -1 \amp 2 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)

    2.2.6.8.

      This gives

    2. It is true, in this case, that \(AB=BA=I\text{.}\)

    2.2.6.9.

    Answer.
    1. False
    2. True
    3. False
    4. True
    5. True
    Solution.
    1. False, the number of components of \(\xvec\) equals the number of columns of \(A\text{.}\)
    2. True. This is a result of Proposition 2.2.4.
    3. False. The shape of \(A\) is \(8\times5\text{.}\)
    4. True, because the augmented matrix \(\left[\begin{array}{r|r} A \amp \bvec \end{array}\right]\) cannot have a pivot position in the rightmost column.
    5. True. Because there is not a pivot position in every row of \(A\text{,}\) the augmented matrix \(\left[\begin{array}{r|r} A \amp \bvec \end{array}\right]\) will have a pivot position in the rightmost column for some vectors \(\bvec\text{.}\)

    2.2.6.10.

    Answer.
    1. \(A \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. No
    3. \(\xvec=\zerovec\text{.}\)
    4. Every equation \(A\xvec=\bvec\) has exactly one solution.
    Solution.
    1. Since the solution is unique, there must be a pivot position in every column of \(A\text{.}\) This means that there are four pivot positions so there is a pivot position in every row. In other words, \(A \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. No, because \(A\) has a pivot in every row.
    3. There is only one solution, which is \(\xvec=\zerovec\text{.}\)
    4. If \(\bvec\) is a four-dimensional vector, then \(A\xvec=\bvec\) has a unique solution since \(A\) has a pivot position in every row and every column. This means that \(\bvec\) can be written as a linear combination of the columns of \(A\) in exactly one way.

    2.2.6.11.

    Answer.
    1. \(\xvec = x_3\threevec{-2}{-1}{1}\text{.}\)
    2. \(\xvec=\threevec{1}{-2}{0}+x_3\threevec{-2}{-1}{1}\text{.}\)
    3. Notice that \(A(\xvec_h + \xvec_p) = A\xvec_h + A\xvec_p = \zerovec + \bvec = \bvec\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We have the augmented matrix
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 2 \amp 4 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp -3 \amp 0 \\ 3 \amp 1 \amp 7 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp 2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      so that \(\xvec = x_3\threevec{-2}{-1}{1}\text{.}\) The solution space is a line that passes through the origin.
    2. Now we have
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 2 \amp 4 \amp -3 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp -3 \amp -4 \\ 3 \amp 1 \amp 7 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp 2 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      so that \(\xvec=\threevec{1}{-2}{0}+x_3\threevec{-2}{-1}{1}\text{.}\) This is a line through \(\threevec{1}{-2}{0}\) parallel to \(\threevec{-2}{-1}{1}\text{.}\) Notice that this line is parallel to the line in part a.
    3. Notice that \(A(\xvec_h + \xvec_p) = A\xvec_h + A\xvec_p = \zerovec + \bvec = \bvec\text{.}\) This shows that if we add a vector from the solution space to \(A\xvec=\zerovec\) to a vector from the solution space to \(A\xvec=\bvec\text{,}\) we obtain a vector in the solution space to \(A\xvec=\bvec\text{.}\)

    1. We see that

    2.2.6.13.

    Answer.
    1. \(A\vvec_1 = \twovec{5}{2} = \vvec_1\) and \(A\vvec_2 = \twovec{-0.3}{0.3} = 0.3\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    2. We have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{rl} \xvec_2=A\xvec_1 = \amp A(c_1\vvec_1+c_2\vvec_2) \\ =\amp c_1A\vvec_1 + c_2A\vvec_2 = c_1\vvec_1 +0.3c_2\vvec_2\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    3. \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{rl} \xvec_3=A\xvec_2=\amp A(c_1\vvec_1+0.3c_2\vvec_2) \\ = \amp c_1A\vvec_1 +0.3c_2A\vvec_2 = c_1\vvec_1 +0.3^2c_2\vvec_2\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
      and so on.
    4. We find that
      \begin{equation*} \xvec_1=\twovec{500}{500} = \frac{1000}{7}\vvec_1 + \frac{1500}{7}\vvec_2\text{.} \end{equation*}
    5. Then
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{l} \xvec_2=\frac{1000}{7}\vvec_1 + 0.3\frac{1500}{7}\vvec{2}, \\ \xvec_3=\frac{1000}{7}\vvec_1 + 0.3^2\frac{1500}{7}\vvec{2}, \\ \xvec_4=\frac{1000}{7}\vvec_1 + 0.3^3\frac{1500}{7}\vvec{2}\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    6. After a very long time, the second term becomes increasingly close to zero and so
      \begin{equation*} \xvec\approx\frac{1000}{7}\vvec_1 = \twovec{5000/7}{2000/7} = \twovec{714.3}{285.7}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    Solution.
    1. \(A\vvec_1 = \twovec{5}{2} = \vvec_1\) and \(A\vvec_2 = \twovec{-0.3}{0.3} = 0.3\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    2. We have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{rl} \xvec_2=A\xvec_1 = \amp A(c_1\vvec_1+c_2\vvec_2) \\ =\amp c_1A\vvec_1 + c_2A\vvec_2 = c_1\vvec_1 +0.3c_2\vvec_2\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    3. \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{rl} \xvec_3=A\xvec_2=\amp A(c_1\vvec_1+0.3c_2\vvec_2) \\ = \amp c_1A\vvec_1 -0.3c_2A\vvec_2 = c_1\vvec_1 +0.3^2c_2\vvec_2\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
      and so on.
    4. We find that
      \begin{equation*} \xvec_1=\twovec{500}{500} = \frac{1000}{7}\vvec_1 + \frac{1500}{7}\vvec_2\text{.} \end{equation*}
    5. Then
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{l} \xvec_2=\frac{1000}{7}\vvec_1 + 0.3\frac{1500}{7}\vvec{2}, \\ \xvec_3=\frac{1000}{7}\vvec_1 + 0.3^2\frac{1500}{7}\vvec{2}, \\ \xvec_4=\frac{1000}{7}\vvec_1 + 0.3^3\frac{1500}{7}\vvec{2}\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    6. After a very long time, the second term becomes increasingly close to zero and so
      \begin{equation*} \xvec\approx\frac{1000}{7}\vvec_1 = \twovec{5000/7}{2000/7} = \twovec{714.3}{285.7}\text{.} \end{equation*}

    2.3 The span of a set of vectors
    2.3.4 Exercises

    2.3.4.1.

    Answer.
    1. \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2} = \real^2\text{.}\)
    2. For the following vectors,
      1. \(\bvec\) is in \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3}\text{.}\)
      2. \(\vvec_3\) is in \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3}\text{.}\)
      3. \(\bvec\) is not in \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3}\text{.}\)
      4. \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3}\) is a plane in \(\real^3\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The equation \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \end{array}\right] ~\xvec = \bvec\) is consistent so \(\bvec\) is in \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2}\text{.}\) Since \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \end{array}\right]\) has a pivot position in every row, \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2}\) is \(\real^2\text{.}\)
    2. Let’s consider the following vectors.
      1. The equation \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr}\vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \amp \vvec_3 \end{array}\right]~\xvec = \bvec\) is consistent so \(\bvec\) is in \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3}\text{.}\)
      2. The vector \(\vvec_3\) is in \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2}\) because \(\vvec_3 = \vvec_1-2\vvec_2\text{.}\)
      3. The equation \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr}\vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \amp \vvec_3 \end{array}\right]~\xvec = \bvec\) is not consistent so \(\bvec\) is not in \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3}\text{.}\)
      4. \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3}\) is the plane defined by \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{.}\)

    Answer.
    1. Yes
    2. Yes
    3. \(\laspan{\zerovec,\zerovec,\ldots,\zerovec}\) consists only of the vector \(\zerovec\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. Yes, because \(\zerovec = 0\vvec_1+0\vvec_2+\ldots+0\vvec_n\text{.}\)
    2. Yes, \(\xvec=\zerovec\) is a solution to the equation \(A\xvec=\zerovec\text{.}\)
    3. \(\laspan{\zerovec,\zerovec,\ldots,\zerovec}\) consists only of the vector \(\zerovec\text{.}\)

    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 3 \amp -6 \\ -2 \amp 2 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right]\) so \(\bvec\) can be any vector in \(\real^2\text{.}\)

    Answer.
    The columns of \(A\) do not span \(\real^4\text{.}\) The columns of \(B\) do.
    Solution.

      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 3 \amp 0 \amp -1 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp -1 \amp 3 \amp 7 \\ 3 \amp -2 \amp 1 \amp 5 \\ -1 \amp 2 \amp 2 \amp 3 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      so the columns of \(A\) do not span \(\real^4\text{.}\)
    2. The reduced row echelon form of \(B\) is
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 3 \amp 0 \amp -1 \amp 4 \\ 1 \amp -1 \amp 3 \amp -1 \\ 3 \amp -2 \amp 1 \amp 3 \\ -1 \amp 2 \amp 2 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      so the columns of \(B\) span \(\real^4\text{.}\)

    2. No
    3. Yes
    Solution.

    2. No. Since the columns of \(A\) span \(\real^3\text{,}\) then the equation \(A\yvec=\bvec\) has a solution \(\yvec\) for every vector \(\bvec\text{.}\) However, we may not be able to solve the equation \(B\xvec=\yvec\) if the columns of \(B\) do not span \(\real^4\text{.}\)
    3. Yes. If we are given a vector \(\bvec\) in \(\real^3\text{,}\) we can find a vector \(\yvec\) such that \(A\yvec = \bvec\) since the columns of \(A\) span \(\real^3\text{.}\) Since the columns of \(B\) span \(\real^4\text{,}\) we can find a vector \(\xvec\) such that \(B\xvec=\yvec\text{.}\) Then \(AB\xvec = \bvec\text{,}\) which means that \(B\) is the span of the columns of \(AB\text{.}\)

    2.3.4.9.

    Answer.
    1. There is a pivot position in each row and each column.
    2. The span is \(\real^{12}\text{.}\)
    3. There is a unique solution.
    4. There is a unique solution \(\xvec=\zerovec.\)
    Solution.

    2. Since there is a pivot position in every row, the span of the columns of \(A\) is \(\real^{12}\text{.}\)
    3. There is a unique solution to the equation because \(A\) has a pivot position in every row and every column.
    4. There is a unique solution \(\xvec=\zerovec\text{.}\)

    2.3.4.10.

    Answer.
    1. Yes
    2. We have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{rl} a\vvec_1+b\vvec_2+c\vvec_3 \amp = a\vvec_1 + b\vvec_2+c(-\vvec_1+2\vvec_2) \\ \amp = (a-c)\vvec_1+(b+2c)\vvec_2\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    3. As seen in the last part of this problem, every linear combination of \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_3\) can be rewritten as a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{.}\)
