    2.3.4.11.

    Answer.
    1. \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3}\) is the plane defined by \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    2. \(2a-4b+c=0\text{.}\)
    3. There would be two equations involving the variables \(a\text{,}\) \(b\text{,}\) and \(c\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. Finding the reduced row echelon form
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 2 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ -2 \amp 0 \amp 2 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      shows that \(\vvec_3\) is a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{.}\) Therefore, \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3}\) is the plane defined by \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    2. We see that
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 2 \amp 1 \amp a \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \amp b \\ -2 \amp 0 \amp 2 \amp c \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 2 \amp 1 \amp a \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \amp b \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 2a-4b+c \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      If the equation is consistent, we must have \(2a-4b+c=0\text{.}\) The solution space is the plane defined by \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    3. In that case, there would be two equations involving the variables \(a\text{,}\) \(b\text{,}\) and \(c\text{,}\) and the solution space would be a line.

    2.4 Linear independence
    2.4.5 Exercises

    2.4.5.1.

    Answer.
    1. Four vectors in \(\real^3\) must always be linearly dependent.
    2. \(\vvec_3 = 2\vvec_1-\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    3. \(c_1=-2\text{,}\) \(c_2=1\text{,}\) \(c_3=1\text{,}\) and \(c_4=0\)
    4. \(\displaystyle \xvec = \fourvec{-2}{1}{1}{0}\)
    Solution.
    1. We view the vectors as the columns of a matrix so that
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 2 \amp -2 \\ 2 \amp 1 \amp 3 \amp 4 \\ 1 \amp 3 \amp -1 \amp -1 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      Since there is a column without a pivot position, the vectors must be linearly dependent. We also expect this because four vectors in \(\real^3\) must always be linearly dependent.
    2. If we view the first three columns as an augmented matrix, we have
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp 2 \\ 2 \amp 1 \amp 3 \\ 1 \amp 3 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      We see that \(\vvec_3 = 2\vvec_1-\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    3. From the solution to the previous part, we have \(-2\vvec_1+\vvec_2+\vvec_3 = \zerovec\text{,}\) which says that \(c_1=-2\text{,}\) \(c_2=1\text{,}\) \(c_3=1\text{,}\) and \(c_4=0\) is an appropriate choice of weights.
    4. From the solution to the previous part, we see that \(\xvec = \fourvec{-2}{1}{1}{0}\) is a solution of the homogeneous equation.

    2.4.5.2.

    Answer.
    1. Linearly independent.
    2. \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3} = \real^3\text{.}\)
    3. Because \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3} = \real^3\text{,}\) every vector in \(\real^3\) can be written as a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_3\text{.}\)
    4. In exactly one way.
    Solution.
    1. Viewing the vectors as the columns of a matrix, we have
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 2 \amp 1 \amp 2 \\ -1 \amp 2 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 3 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      Since there is a pivot position in every column, the vectors are linearly independent.
    2. Since the matrix has a pivot position in every row, we see that \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3} = \real^3\text{.}\)
    3. Because \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3} = \real^3\text{,}\) every vector in \(\real^3\) can be written as a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_3\text{.}\)
    4. In exactly one way. Because the matrix has a pivot position in every column, there cannot be a free variable.

    2.4.5.3.

    Answer.
    1. Yes
    2. Any subset is linearly independent.
    3. They form a linearly independent set.
    Solution.
    1. Yes. If \(c_1\vvec_1 + c_2\vvec_2 = \zerovec\) and one of the weights is not zero, we can rearrange this expression to solve for one of the vectors as a scalar multiple of the other. For instance, if \(2\vvec_1-3\vvec_2=\zerovec\text{,}\) then \(\vvec_1 = \frac32\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    2. Any subset of the vectors forms a linearly independent set. Because the original set is linearly independent, none of the vectors can be written as a linear combination of the others. This is still true when we look at a smaller set of the vectors.
    3. The set of vectors \(\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\ldots,\vvec_n,\bvec\) will also be linearly independent. Since \(A\xvec=\bvec\) is inconsistent, we know that \(\bvec\) cannot be written as a linear combination of the other vectors. We also know that each of the vectors \(\vvec_j\) cannot be written as a linear combination of the others because \(\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\ldots,\vvec_n\) is a linearly independent set.

    2.4.5.4.

    Answer.
    1. False
    2. True
    3. False
    4. True
    5. True
    Solution.
    1. False. We only know that one vector can be written as a linear combination of the others.
    2. True. If we put the vectors into a matrix, there are more columns than rows. Therefore, there must be a column without a pivot position so the vectors form a linearly dependent set.
    3. False. They could form a linearly independent set, but we cannot guarantee it. We would have to look at the location of the pivot positions in the associated \(10\times5\) matrix.
    4. True. In this case, \(\vvec_2\) can be written as a linear combination of the other vectors so the set is linearly dependent.
    5. True. Since the vectors are linearly independent, \(A\) has a pivot position in every column. Therefore, there is not a free variable in the description of the solution space to the equation \(A\xvec=\bvec\text{.}\) Therefore, the solution is unique.

    2.4.5.5.

    Answer.
    1. \(\displaystyle \xvec=\fourvec{2}{-1}{3}{1}\)
    2. There are infinitely many solutions to the homogeneous equation \(A\xvec=\zerovec\text{.}\)
    3. \(\vvec_4 = -2\vvec_1 + \vvec_2 - 3\vvec_3\text{.}\)
    4. One vector can be written as a linear combination of the others.
    Solution.
    1. The vector \(\xvec=\fourvec{2}{-1}{3}{1}\) is a solution.
    2. There is a nonzero solution to the homogeneous equation \(A\xvec=\zerovec\text{.}\) Since there is also the zero solution \(\xvec=\zerovec\text{,}\) there must be infinitely many solutions. This can only happen when there is a column of \(A\) that does not have a pivot position.
    3. \(\vvec_4 = -2\vvec_1 + \vvec_2 - 3\vvec_3\text{.}\)
    4. Because one of the vectors can be written as a linear combination of the others, the set of vectors is linearly dependent.

    2.4.5.6.

    Answer.
    1. There must be at least 27 vectors.
    2. There must be at most 27 vectors.
    3. There are exactly 27 vectors.
    4. There is exactly one solution.
    Solution.
    1. In this case, the matrix \(A\) must have a pivot position in evey row. There must be at least 27 vectors for this to be possible.
    2. In this case, the matrix \(A\) must have a pivot position in every column. There must be at most 27 vectors for this to be possible.
    3. There must be exactly 27 vectors.
    4. There is exactly one solution to the equation \(A\xvec=\bvec\) because the matrix \(A\) has a pivot position in every row and every column.

    2.4.5.7.

    Answer.
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. This is not possible.
    4. This is not possible.
    5. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 4 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. There must be a pivot position in every column: \(\left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. There must be a pivot position in every row. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. This is not possible. The matrix \(A\) has dimensions \(4\times 3\) so there cannot be a pivot position in every row.
    4. This is not possible. The matrix \(A\) has dimensions \(3\times5\) so there cannot be a pivot position in every column.
    5. There must be a pivot position in every row. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 4 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)

    2.4.5.8.

    Answer.
    1. They are linearly dependent.
    2. The columns of \(B\) and \(C\) are all equal.
    Solution.
    1. Since \(B\neq0\text{,}\) there is a column of \(B\text{,}\) which we’ll call \(\xvec\text{,}\) that is not zero. Since \(AB = 0\text{,}\) we know that \(A\xvec = \zerovec\text{,}\) which means that \(\xvec\) is a nonzero solution to the homogenous equation \(A\xvec = \zerovec\text{.}\) Therefore, the columns of \(A\) are linearly dependent.
    2. Since the columns of \(A\) are linearly independent, the only solution to the homogeneous equation \(A\xvec=\zerovec\) is \(\xvec=\zerovec\text{.}\) Since that \(A(B-C) = 0\text{,}\) every column \(\xvec\) of \(B-C\) satisfies \(A\xvec = \zerovec\text{.}\) Therefore, every column of \(B-C\) is the zero vector, which implies that \(B=C\text{.}\)

    2.4.5.9.

    Answer.
    1. \(k=3\text{.}\)
    2. \(k\neq3\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We construct the matrix
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 2 \amp 4 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -2 \amp 2 \\ 1 \amp -1 \amp k \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp k-3 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      If the vectors are linearly dependent, we cannot have a pivot position in the third column. This means that \(k=3\text{.}\)
    2. If the vectors span \(\real^3\text{,}\) there must be a pivot in the third row, which means that \(k\neq 3\text{.}\)

    2.4.5.10.

    Answer.
    1. Any linear combination of \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) and \(\wvec\) can be rewritten as a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    2. \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3,\vvec_4} = \laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_4}\text{.}\)
    3. \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3,\vvec_4} = \laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2}\text{.}\)
    4. It is a plane in \(\real^3\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. If \(\wvec\) is a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) then we can write \(\wvec=a\vvec_1+b\vvec_2\text{.}\) Then any linear combination of \(\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\wvec\) can be rewritten as
      \begin{equation*} c_1\vvec_1+c_2\vvec_2+c_3\wvec=(c_1+c_3a)\vvec_1 + (c_2+c_3b)\vvec_2\text{.} \end{equation*}
    2. We have
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 2 \amp 1 \amp -2 \amp 7 \\ -1 \amp 2 \amp 6 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 2 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp -2 \amp 3 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 2 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      This shows that \(\vvec_3 = -2\vvec_1+2\vvec_2\) and so \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3,\vvec_4} = \laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_4}\text{.}\)
    3. The remaining three vectors are linearly dependent because \(\vvec_4 = 3\vvec_1+\vvec_2\text{.}\) We therefore have \(\laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2,\vvec_3,\vvec_4} = \laspan{\vvec_1,\vvec_2}\text{.}\)
    4. The vectors \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\) are linearly independent so their span is a plane in \(\real^3\text{.}\)

    2.5 Matrix transformations
    2.5.5 Exercises

    2.5.5.1.

    Answer.
    1. \(T:\real^3\to\real^3\) and \(S:\real^3\to\real^2\text{.}\)
    2. \(\threevec{6}{-1}{-6}\text{.}\)
    3. \(\twovec{-4}{0}\text{.}\)
    4. \(\twovec{7}{-1}\text{.}\)
    5. \(\left[ \begin{array}{rrr} 2 \amp -3 \amp -2 \\ 5 \amp 6 \amp 6 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. Since \(A\) is a \(3\times3\) matrix, we have \(T:\real^3\to\real^3\text{.}\) Since \(B\) is a \(2\times3\) matrix, we have \(S:\real^3\to\real^2\text{.}\)
    2. \(T\left(\threevec{1}{-3}{2}\right) = A\threevec{1}{-3}{2} = \threevec{6}{-1}{-6}\text{.}\)
    3. \(S\left(\threevec{-2}{2}{1}\right) = A\threevec{-2}{2}{1}= \twovec{-4}{0}\text{.}\)
    4. \(S\circ T\left(\threevec{1}{-3}{2}\right) = BA\threevec{1}{-3}{2} = \twovec{7}{-1}\text{.}\)
    5. \(C = BA = \left[ \begin{array}{rrr} 2 \amp -3 \amp -2 \\ 5 \amp 6 \amp 6 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)

    2.5.5.2.

    Answer.
    1. \(A=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 3 \amp -1 \amp 4 \\ 0 \amp 5 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    2. Because the first component has the term \(3x_1^4\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The matrix \(A=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 3 \amp -1 \amp 4 \\ 0 \amp 5 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    2. Because the first component has the term \(3x_1^4\text{,}\) there is no matrix such that \(T(\xvec) = A\xvec\text{.}\)

    2.5.5.3.

    Answer.
    1. \(\xvec= x_3\threevec{2}{-1}{1}\text{.}\)
    2. There are no such vectors.
    3. \(\xvec=\threevec{-2}{-2}{0} + x_3\threevec{2}{-1}{1}\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We have \(T(\xvec) = A\xvec = \zerovec\text{.}\) The reduced row echelon form of \(A\) is \(A \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\) Therefore, the solutions have the form \(\xvec=x_3\threevec{2}{-1}{1}\text{.}\)
    2. We see that
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 3 \amp 1 \amp -8 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 5 \amp 9 \\ 0 \amp 2 \amp 2 \amp 2 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 0 \amp -2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      Since this is an inconsistent system, there are no vectors \(\xvec\) satisfying the equation.
    3. In the same way, we have
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr|r} 1 \amp 3 \amp 1 \amp -8 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 5 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 2 \amp 2 \amp -4 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp -2 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      This shows that \(\xvec=\threevec{-2}{-2}{0} + x_3\threevec{2}{-1}{1}\text{.}\)

    2.5.5.4.

    Answer.
    1. \(\twovec{1}{-1}\text{.}\)
    2. \(\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    3. \(\xvec=x_3\threevec{1}{1}{1}\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. \(T\left(\threevec{2}{1}{2}\right) = 2\vvec_1+\vvec_2+2\vvec_3 = \twovec{1}{-1}\text{.}\)
    2. \(T\left(\threevec{0}{1}{0}\right) = \vvec_2\text{.}\)
    3. The matrix that defines \(T\) is
      \begin{equation*} A = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \amp \vvec_3 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 2 \amp -1 \amp -1 \\ 1 \amp 1 \amp -2 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      This shows that the solutions to \(T(\xvec) = A\xvec = \zerovec\) are \(\xvec=x_3\threevec{1}{1}{1}\text{.}\)

    2.5.5.5.

    Answer.
      1. We have \(T(\xvec) = \threevec{10x_1+20x_2}{50x_1+20x_2}{30x_1+30x_2} \text{.}\)
      2. \(10x_1 + 20x_2\text{.}\)
      3. Bakery 1 makes 10 cakes per hour and Bakery 2 makes 20.
      1. \(0.6x_1 + 0.7x_2 \text{.}\)
      2. \(\displaystyle 0.4x_1 + 0.3x_2\)
      3. \(T(\xvec) = \twovec{0.6x_1+0.7x_2}{0.4x_1+0.3x_2} \text{.}\)
      4. \(A = \begin{bmatrix} 0.6 \amp 0.7 \\ 0.4 \amp 0.3 \\ \end{bmatrix} \text{.}\)
    Solution.
      1. We have
        \begin{equation*} T(\xvec) = \begin{bmatrix} 10 \amp 20 \\ 50 \amp 30 \\ 30 \amp 30 \\ \end{bmatrix} \twovec{x_1}{x_2} = \threevec{10x_1+20x_2}{50x_1+20x_2}{30x_1+30x_2}. \end{equation*}
      2. The number of cakes produced is \(10x_1 + 20x_2\text{.}\)
      3. This is consistent with the given information because Bakery 1 produces cakes at the rate of 10 cakes per hour so the number of cakes it produces is \(10x_1\text{.}\) In the same way, the number of cakes produced by Bakery 2 is \(20x_2\) giving a total of \(10x_1 + 20x_2\text{.}\)
      1. Of the bicycles that begin at \(P\text{,}\) 60% of them end up at \(P\text{.}\) Therefore \(0.6x_1\) is the number of bicycles that begin at \(P\) and end at \(P\text{.}\) Similarly, 70% of the bicycles that begin at \(Q\) end up at \(P\) so this number is \(0.7x_2\text{.}\) Therefore, the total number of bicycles that end up at \(P\) is
        \begin{equation*} 0.6x_1 + 0.7x_2. \end{equation*}
      2. In the same way, the number of bicycles that end up at \(Q\) is
        \begin{equation*} 0.4x_1 + 0.3x_2. \end{equation*}
      3. This gives the matrix
        \begin{equation*} T(\xvec) = \twovec{0.6x_1+0.7x_2}{0.4x_1+0.3x_2} = \begin{bmatrix} 0.6 \amp 0.7 \\ 0.4 \amp 0.3 \\ \end{bmatrix} \xvec. \end{equation*}
      4. This expression leads to \(A = \begin{bmatrix} 0.6 \amp 0.7 \\ 0.4 \amp 0.3 \\ \end{bmatrix} \text{.}\)

    2.5.5.6.

    Answer.
    1. False
    2. True
    3. False
    4. True
    Solution.
    1. False. The dimensions of \(A\) are \(5\times4\text{.}\)
    2. True. The dimensions of \(A\) are \(2\times3\) so there must be a column without a pivot position.
    3. False. The dimensions of \(A\) are \(3\times2\) so there must be a row without a pivot position.
    4. True. The vector \(\xvec=\zerovec\) is always a solution.

    2.5.5.7.

    Answer.
    1. \(A = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 20 \amp 30 \amp 0 \\ 15 \amp 5 \amp 40 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    2. \(B = \left[\begin{array}{rr} 5 \amp 6 \\ 8 \amp 10 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    3. \(\twovec{M}{Y}=T\left(\threevec{30}{20}{10}\right) = \twovec{1200}{950}\) and \(\twovec{E}{L}=S\left(T\left(\threevec{30}{20}{10}\right)\right) = \twovec{11700}{19100}\text{.}\)
    4. \(C=BA = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 190 \amp 180 \amp 240 \\ 310 \amp 290 \amp 400 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The first column of the matrix is \(T\left(\threevec{1}{0}{0}\right)=\twovec{20}{15}\text{.}\) This shows us that the matrix is \(A = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 20 \amp 30 \amp 0 \\ 15 \amp 5 \amp 40 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    2. In the same way, the matrix is \(B = \left[\begin{array}{rr} 5 \amp 6 \\ 8 \amp 10 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    3. \(\twovec{M}{Y}=T\left(\threevec{30}{20}{10}\right) = \twovec{1200}{950}\) and \(\twovec{E}{L}=S\left(T\left(\threevec{30}{20}{10}\right)\right) = \twovec{11700}{19100}\text{.}\)
    4. \(C=BA = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 190 \amp 180 \amp 240 \\ 310 \amp 290 \amp 400 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)

    2.5.5.8.

    Answer.
    1. \(T\left(\twovec{1}{0}\right) = \twovec{1}{-2} \text{.}\)
    2. \(A=\left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 2 \\ -2 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    3. \(T\left(\twovec{4}{-5}\right) = \twovec{-6}{-8}\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We first find weights \(c\) and \(d\) such that \(\twovec{1}{0} = c\twovec{1}{1} + d\twovec{-1}{1}\) by constructing the augmented matrix
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp -1 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 1 \amp 0 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp \frac{1}{2} \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -\frac{1}{2} \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      This shows that \(\twovec{1}{0} = \frac12\twovec{1}{1} - \frac12\twovec{-1}{1}\text{.}\) Therefore,
      \begin{equation*} T\left(\twovec{1}{0}\right) = A\twovec10 = \frac12 A\twovec11 - \frac12A\twovec{-1}{1}= \twovec{1}{-2}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    2. In the same way, we find that
      \begin{equation*} T\left(\twovec{0}{1}\right) = \frac12A\twovec{1}{1} + \frac12A\twovec{-1}{1} = \twovec{2}{0}\text{.} \end{equation*}
      This gives the matrix \(A=\left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 2 \\ -2 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    3. Then \(T\left(\twovec{4}{-5}\right) = A\twovec{4}{-5} = \twovec{-6}{-8}\text{.}\)

    2.5.5.9.

    Answer.
    1. \(\twovec{1.4}{3.1}\text{.}\)
    2. \(\twovec{0.8}{1.2}\text{.}\)
    3. \(\twovec{6.8}{15.9}\text{.}\)
    4. \(\twovec{116.8}{272.5}\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    The initial state is the vector \(\xvec_0=\twovec{1}{2}\text{.}\)
    1. At the beginning of August, we have \(\xvec_1 = A\xvec_0 = \twovec{1.4}{3.1}\text{.}\)
    2. At the beginning of June, we have \(A\xvec_{-1} = \xvec_0\text{.}\) Solving this equation gives \(\xvec_{-1}=\twovec{0.8}{1.2}\text{.}\)
    3. At the beginning of December, we have \(\xvec_5=A^5\xvec_0 = \twovec{6.8}{15.9}\text{.}\)
    4. At the beginning of July in the following year, we have \(\xvec_{12} = A^{12}\xvec_0 = \twovec{116.8}{272.5}\text{.}\)

    2.5.5.10.

    Answer.
    1. \(\displaystyle T\left(\twovec{1000}{0}\right) = \twovec{950}{0}\)
    2. \(\displaystyle T\left(\twovec{0}{1000}\right) = \twovec{500}{500}\)
    3. \(A=\left[\begin{array}{rr}0.95 \amp 0.50 \\ 0.05 \amp 0.50 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    4. There are 1665 students present and 135 absent.
    5. 1778
    6. 1636
    7. It stabilizes around 1636 students present.
    Solution.
    1. \(T\left(\twovec{1000}{0}\right) = \twovec{950}{0}\) so that \(T\left(\twovec{1}{0}\right) = \twovec{0.95}{0}\text{.}\)
    2. \(T\left(\twovec{0}{1000}\right) = \twovec{500}{500}\) so that \(T\left(\twovec{1}{0}\right) = \twovec{0.5}{0.5}\text{.}\)
    3. The matrix \(A=\left[\begin{array}{rr}0.95 \amp 0.50 \\ 0.05 \amp 0.50 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    4. \(T(\xvec) = A\xvec = \twovec{1665}{135}\) meaning that 1665 students are present and 135 are absent.
    5. We want to solve the equation \(T(\xvec) = A\xvec=\twovec{1700}{100}\text{,}\) which gives \(\xvec = \twovec{1778}{22}\text{.}\) There were 1778 students present on Monday.
    6. The following Tuesday, the state will be described by the vector \(A^7\twovec{1700}{100} = \twovec{1636}{163}\text{.}\) This means that there were 1636 students present the following Tuesday.
    7. If we compute \(A^k\twovec{1700}{100}\) for some large powers \(k\text{,}\) we find that there are about 1636 students present every day after a very long time.

    2.6 The geometry of matrix transformations
    2.6.4 Exercises

    2.6.4.1.

    Answer.
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp -1 \\ -1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac 12 \amp -\frac{\sqrt{3}}2 \\ \frac{\sqrt{3}}2 \amp \frac12\\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    5. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac{\sqrt{3}}2 \amp \frac12\\ \frac 12 \amp -\frac{\sqrt{3}}2 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    We create the following matrices as \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} T(\evec_1) \amp T(\evec_2) \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp -1 \\ -1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos(60^\circ) \amp -\sin(60^\circ) \\ \sin(60^\circ) \amp \cos(60^\circ) \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac 12 \amp -\frac{\sqrt{3}}2 \\ \frac{\sqrt{3}}2 \amp \frac12\\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    5. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac 12 \amp -\frac{\sqrt{3}}2 \\ \frac{\sqrt{3}}2 \amp \frac12\\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac{\sqrt{3}}2 \amp \frac12\\ \frac 12 \amp -\frac{\sqrt{3}}2 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)

    2.6.4.2.

    Answer.
    1. This is the same as a counterclockwise \(90^\circ\) rotation.
    2. This is the same as a clockwise \(90^\circ\) rotation.
    3. This is the same as a \(180^\circ\) rotation.
    4. This is the same as a reflection in the horizontal axis.
    Solution.
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp -1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\) This is the same as a counterclockwise \(90^\circ\) rotation.
    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ -1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\) This is the same as a clockwise \(90^\circ\) rotation.
    3. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp -1 \\ -1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\) This is the same as a \(180^\circ\) rotation.
    4. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp -1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\) This is the same as a reflection in the horizontal axis.

    2.6.4.3.

    Answer.
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ -1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ -1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    We construct the matrices as \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} T(\evec_1) \amp T(\evec_2) \amp T(\evec_3) \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ -1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ -1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ -1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{,}\) which is a \(-90^\circ\) rotation about the \(z\)-axis.

    2.6.4.4.

    Answer.
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ -1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. The square of the matrix \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\) is the identity.
    3. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp \frac13 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The transformation \(S\) should rotate vectors by \(-90^\circ\text{.}\) The matrix is therefore \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ -1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
      To check \(S\circ T\text{,}\) we compute \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ -1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp -1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. The matrix defining the reflection is \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\) Multiplying this by itself gives the identity.
    3. We should apply a shear in the opposite direction: \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. We will undo the stretch in the horizontal and vertical directions with the matrix \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp \frac13 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)

    2.6.4.5.

    Answer.
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. \(\displaystyle \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp -1 \amp 3 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 3 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right].\)
    5. The point \((-10,5)\) is rotated to \((-2,-9)\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. \(\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. \(\displaystyle \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp -1 \amp 3 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 3 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right].\)
    5. If we call this matrix \(C\text{,}\) we compute \(C\threevec{-10}{5}{1} = \threevec{-2}{-9}{1}\text{.}\) Therefore, the point \((-10,5)\) is rotated to \((-2,-9)\text{.}\)

    2.6.4.6.

    Answer.
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    4. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\) The result of composing the two projections sends every vector to \(\zerovec\text{.}\)
    4. \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)

    2.6.4.7.

    Answer.
    1. It stretches vectors by \(r\text{.}\)
    2. This matrix rotates vectors by an angle \(\theta\text{.}\)
    3. No matter which order we multiply the matrices, we find that their product is
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr} r\cos\theta \amp -r\sin\theta \\ r\sin\theta \amp r\sin\theta \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
    4. This follows from the previous part.
    5. If we use polar coordinates to write \(\twovec{a}{b} = \twovec{r\cos\theta}{r\sin\theta}\text{,}\) multiplying a vector by \(A\) will stretch the vector by \(r\) and rotate it by \(\theta\text{.}\)
    6. If
      \begin{equation*} \twovec{a}{b} = \twovec{r\cos\theta}{r\sin\theta}, \twovec{c}{d} = \twovec{s\cos\phi}{s\sin\phi}\text{,} \end{equation*}
      then \(S\circ T\) has the effect of stretching a vector by \(rs\) and rotating it by \(\theta + \phi\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The matrix \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} r \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp r \\ \end{array}\right]\) stretches vectors by \(r\text{.}\) It has the same effect as scalar multiplication by \(r\text{.}\)
    2. This matrix rotates vectors by an angle \(\theta\text{.}\)
    3. No matter which order we multiply the matrices, we find that their product is
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr} r \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp r \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos\theta \amp -\sin\theta \\ \sin\theta \amp \sin\theta \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} r\cos\theta \amp -r\sin\theta \\ r\sin\theta \amp r\sin\theta \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
    4. This follows from the previous part.
    5. If we use polar coordinates to write \(\twovec{a}{b} = \twovec{r\cos\theta}{r\sin\theta}\text{,}\) multiplying a vector by \(A\) will stretch the vector by \(r\) and rotate it by \(\theta\text{.}\)
    6. If
      \begin{equation*} \twovec{a}{b} = \twovec{r\cos\theta}{r\sin\theta}, \twovec{c}{d} = \twovec{s\cos\phi}{s\sin\phi}\text{,} \end{equation*}
      then \(S\circ T\) has the effect of stretching a vector by \(rs\) and rotating it by \(\theta + \phi\text{.}\)

    2.6.4.8.

    Answer.
    1. We have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{rl} \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos\theta \amp -\sin\theta \\ \sin\theta \amp \cos\theta \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] \amp \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos\theta \amp \sin\theta \\ -\sin\theta \amp \cos\theta \\ \end{array}\right] \\ = \amp \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos(2\theta) \amp \sin(2\theta) \\ \sin(2\theta) \amp -\cos(2\theta) \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    2. Compute that
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos(2\theta) \amp \sin(2\theta) \\ \sin(2\theta) \amp -\cos(2\theta) \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos(2\theta) \amp -\sin(2\theta) \\ \sin(2\theta) \amp \cos(2\theta) \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
    Solution.
    1. We have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{rl} \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos\theta \amp -\sin\theta \\ \sin\theta \amp \cos\theta \\ \end{array}\right] \amp \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos\theta \amp \sin\theta \\ -\sin\theta \amp \cos\theta \\ \end{array}\right] \\ = \amp \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos\theta \amp -\sin\theta \\ \sin\theta \amp \cos\theta \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos\theta \amp \sin\theta \\ \sin\theta \amp -\cos\theta \\ \end{array}\right] \\ = \amp \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos(2\theta) \amp \sin(2\theta) \\ \sin(2\theta) \amp -\cos(2\theta) \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    2. Compute that
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos(2\theta) \amp \sin(2\theta) \\ \sin(2\theta) \amp -\cos(2\theta) \\ \end{array}\right] \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} \cos(2\theta) \amp -\sin(2\theta) \\ \sin(2\theta) \amp \cos(2\theta) \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}

    3 Invertibility, bases, and coordinate systems
    3.1 Invertibility
    3.1.5 Exercises

    3.1.5.1.

    Answer.
    1. \(\displaystyle A\sim I\)
    2. \(\displaystyle A^{-1} = \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} -\frac{1}{2} \amp 0 \amp -\frac{1}{2} \amp \frac{1}{2} \\ 4 \amp -2 \amp 9 \amp 2 \\ -\frac{7}{2} \amp 2 \amp -\frac{15}{2} \amp -\frac{3}{2} \\ \frac{5}{2} \amp -1 \amp \frac{9}{2} \amp \frac{1}{2} \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    3. \(\xvec= \fourvec{-\frac12}{-21}{\frac{35}2}{-\frac{17}{2}} \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We see that \(A\sim I\text{,}\) the identity matrix, which implies that \(A\) has an inverse.
    2. We have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{l} \left[\begin{array}{rrrr|rrrr} 3 \amp -1 \amp 1 \amp 4 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 2 \amp 3 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp -2 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 3 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 2 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \\ \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrr|rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp -\frac{1}{2} \amp 0 \amp -\frac{1}{2} \amp \frac{1}{2} \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 4 \amp -2 \amp 9 \amp 2 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp -\frac{7}{2} \amp 2 \amp -\frac{15}{2} \amp -\frac{3}{2} \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp \frac{5}{2} \amp -1 \amp \frac{9}{2} \amp \frac{1}{2} \end{array}\right]\text{,} \end{array} \end{equation*}
      which says that
      \begin{equation*} A^{-1} = \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} -\frac{1}{2} \amp 0 \amp -\frac{1}{2} \amp \frac{1}{2} \\ 4 \amp -2 \amp 9 \amp 2 \\ -\frac{7}{2} \amp 2 \amp -\frac{15}{2} \amp -\frac{3}{2} \\ \frac{5}{2} \amp -1 \amp \frac{9}{2} \amp \frac{1}{2} \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
    3. We compute that \(\xvec=A^{-1}\fourvec{3}{2}{-3}{-1} = \fourvec{-\frac12}{-21}{\frac{35}2}{-\frac{17}{2}} \text{.}\)

    3.1.5.2.

    Answer.
    1. \(A=\left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp -\frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ \end{array}\right]\) and \(A^{-1}=\left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ -\frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    2. \(A=\left[\begin{array}{rr} -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    3. \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right], \left[\begin{array}{rr} -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right], \left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
    Solution.
    1. \(A=\left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp -\frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\) To undo the rotation, we will perform a \(45^\circ\) clockwise rotation, which is defined by the matrix \(A^{-1}=\left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ -\frac1{\sqrt{2}} \amp \frac1{\sqrt{2}} \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\)
    2. \(A=\left[\begin{array}{rr} -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.}\) We see that \(A^2=I\) because rotating by \(180^\circ\) is its own inverse.
    3. We can do this by constructing matrices that define reflections, such as
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right], \left[\begin{array}{rr} -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right], \left[\begin{array}{rr} 0 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}

    3.1.5.3.

    Answer.
      1. \(D^{-1} = \begin{bmatrix} \frac12 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp \frac14 \\ \end{bmatrix} \text{.}\)
      2. When the entries on the diagonal are all nonzero.
      3. Consider the steps performed in row reducing \(D\) augmented by \(I\text{.}\)
      1. \(L^{-1} = \begin{bmatrix} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ -11 \amp 4 \amp 1 \end{bmatrix} \text{.}\)
      2. Consider the steps in row reducing \(L\) augmented by \(I\text{.}\)
    Solution.
      1. \(D^{-1} = \begin{bmatrix} \frac12 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp \frac14 \\ \end{bmatrix} \text{.}\)
      2. When the entries on the diagonal are all nonzero.
      3. Because the process of row reducing \(D\) augmented by \(I\) can be performed using only scalings. Then the diagonal entries of \(D\) and \(D^{-1}\) are reciprocals of one another.
      1. \(L^{-1} = \begin{bmatrix} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ -11 \amp 4 \amp 1 \end{bmatrix} \text{.}\)
      2. \(L^{-1}\) is always lower triangular because the only row operations needed to row reduce \(L\) augmented by \(I\) are replacements in which a multiple of one row is added to a row underneath it.

    3.1.5.4.

    Answer.
    1. \(BA=I_2\text{.}\)
    2. \(\displaystyle \xvec= \twovec{2}{-1}\)
    3. \(CA=I_2\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We compute that \(BA=I_2\text{.}\)
    2. We find \(\xvec=B\threevec{-1}{-3}{6} = \twovec{2}{-1}\text{,}\) which is indeed a solution to the equation \(A\xvec=\threevec{-1}{-3}{6}\text{.}\)
    3. In the same way, we compute \(CA=I_2\text{.}\)

    3.1.5.5.

    Answer.
    1. \(A^{-1}= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp -\frac13 \amp 0 \\ \frac12 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. \(A^{-1} = \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -6 \amp 3 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 12 \amp -6 \amp -2 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. \(A^{-1} = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -\frac12 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp \frac12 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. If
      \begin{equation*} P = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right], S_1 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} -\frac13 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right], S_2 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp \frac12 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{,} \end{equation*}
      then \(S_2S_1P A = I\) so that \(A^{-1}=S_2S_1P= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 0 \amp -\frac13 \amp 0 \\ \frac12 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    2. We use three replacement operations
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{l} L_1 = \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right], L_2 = \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 3 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right], \\ L_3 = \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp -2 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
      Therefore, \(L_3L_2L_1A = I\) so \(A^{-1} = L_3L_2L_1 = \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -6 \amp 3 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 12 \amp -6 \amp -2 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. We use a scaling followed by replacement operations:
      \begin{equation*} S = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp \frac12 \\ \end{array}\right], U_1 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right], U_2 = \begin{bmatrix} 1 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{bmatrix}, U_3 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      This gives \(U_3U_2U_1SA = I\) so that \(A^{-1} = U_3U_2U_1S = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp -1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -\frac12 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp \frac12 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)

    3.1.5.6.

    Answer.
    1. If \(A^2\) has inverse \(B\text{,}\) then \(A^2B = I\text{.}\) This means that \(A(AB)=I\) so \(A\) is invertible with inverse \(AB\text{.}\)
    2. In the same way, we have \(A^{100}B = A(A^{99}B)=I\text{,}\) which shows that \(A^{-1}=A^{99}B\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. If \(A^2\) has inverse \(B\text{,}\) then \(A^2B = I\text{.}\) This means that \(A(AB)=I\) so \(A\) is invertible with inverse \(AB\text{.}\)
    2. In the same way, we have \(A^{100}B = A(A^{99}B)=I\text{,}\) which shows that \(A^{-1}=A^{99}B\text{.}\)

    3.1.5.7.

    Answer.
    1. True
    2. False
    3. True
    4. False
    5. True
    Solution.
    1. True. If \(A\) is invertible, then it has a pivot position in every column, which implies that the columns are linearly independent.
    2. False. We only know this if \(A\) is a triangular matrix. For instance, the matrix \(\left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right]\) is not invertible.
    3. True. If \(A\) is invertible, then it has a pivot position in every row, which implies that the columns span \(\real^n\text{.}\)
    4. False. Since there is a pivot position in every column, the homogeneous equation \(A\xvec=\zerovec\) has only the zero solution \(\xvec=\zerovec\text{.}\)
    5. True. In this case, the columns of \(A\) span \(\real^n\) so there must be a pivot position in every row. Because \(A\) is a square matrix, it must be row equivalent to the identity matrix \(I\text{.}\)

    3.1.5.8.

    Answer.
    1. No
    2. No
    3. Yes
    4. The span is \(\real^n\text{.}\)
    5. Yes
    Solution.
    1. No. If \(A\) has two identical columns, then the columns are not linearly independent. This means there is a column without a pivot position and so \(A\) is not row equivalent to the identity matrix \(I\text{.}\)
    2. No. If we perform a replacement operation that multiplies one of the equal rows by \(-1\) and adds it to the other equal row, we obtain a matrix having a row whose entries are all zero. This says that the reduced row echelon form has such a row as well. Therefore, there is a row that does not contain a pivot position so the reduced row echelon form cannot be the identity \(I\text{.}\)
    3. Yes. If we multiply both sides of \(AB=AC\) by \(A^{-1}\) on the left, then we see that \(B=C\text{.}\)
    4. The inverse \(A^{-1}\) is also invertible since \(A\) is its inverse. Therefore, the span of the columns of \(A^{-1}\) is \(\real^n\text{.}\)
    5. Yes. Since \(A\) is row equivalent to the identity matrix \(I\text{,}\) the matrix \(B\) is as well. Therefore, \(B\) is invertible.

    3.1.5.9.

    Answer.
    1. We have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{cc} E_1=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right], \amp E_2=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 4 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right], \\ E_3 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp \frac{1}{2} \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right], \amp E_4 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -2 \amp 1 \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    2. \(E = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -1 \amp \frac{1}{2} \amp 0 \\ 6 \amp -1 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. \(A= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 3 \amp 2 \amp -1 \\ 6 \amp 6 \amp 4 \\ -12 \amp -6 \amp 6 \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{cc} E_1=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -2 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right], \amp E_2=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 4 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right], \\ E_3 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp \frac{1}{2} \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right], \amp E_4 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \amp 1 \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
    2. We have \(E = E_4E_3E_2E_1 = \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -1 \amp \frac{1}{2} \amp 0 \\ 5 \amp -\frac12 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    3. We write \(A=E^{-1}U= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 3 \amp 2 \amp -1 \\ 6 \amp 6 \amp 4 \\ -12 \amp -7 \amp 3 \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)

    3.1.5.10.

    Answer.
    1. \(B^2 = PA^2P^{-1}\text{.}\)
    2. \(B^{-1} = PA^{-1}P^{-1}\text{.}\)
    3. \(B^{-1}= PA^{-1}P^{-1}\text{.}\)
    4. \(C = (QP)A(QP)^{-1}\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. \(B^2 = (PAP^{-1})(PAP^{-1}) = PA^2P^{-1}\text{.}\)
    2. \(B (PA^{-1}P^{-1}) = (PAP^{-1})(PA^{-1}P^{-1}) = I\text{.}\)
    3. \(B^{-1}=(PAP^{-1})^{-1} = PA^{-1}P^{-1}\text{.}\)
    4. \(C = QBQ^{-1}=QPAP^{-1}Q^{-1} = (QP)A(QP)^{-1}\text{.}\)

    3.1.5.11.

    Answer.
      1. \(AB\sim I\text{.}\)
      2. If \(B\xvec=\zerovec\text{,}\) then \(AB\xvec = \zerovec\text{.}\)
      3. We now know that \(B\sim I\text{.}\)
      1. The span of the columns of \(AB\) is \(\real^n\text{.}\)
      2. A solution to \(AB\xvec = \bvec\) produces a solution to \(A\xvec = \bvec\text{.}\)
      3. The span of the columns of \(A\) is \(\real^n\text{.}\)
    Solution.
      1. Since \(AB\) is invertible, \(AB\sim I\text{,}\) which says that there is pivot position in every column. Therefore, \(\xvec=\zerovec\) is the only solution to the equation \(AB\xvec = \zerovec\text{.}\)
      2. If \(\xvec\) is a solution to the equation \(B\xvec = \zerovec\text{,}\) then \(AB\xvec = A\zerovec = \zerovec\text{,}\) which says that \(\xvec = \zerovec\text{.}\)
      3. Since the only solution to the homogeneous equation \(B\xvec = \zerovec\) is the zero solution, we know that \(B\sim I\) so \(B\) is invertible.
      1. Since \(AB\) is invertible, the span of the columns of \(AB\) is \(\real^n\text{,}\) which says that every equation \(AB\xvec = \bvec\) is consistent.
      2. If \(\yvec\) is the solution to the equation \(AB\xvec = \bvec\text{,}\) then \(B\yvec\) satisfies \(A(B\yvec) = \bvec\text{,}\) which means that \(A\xvec = \bvec\) is consistent.
      3. We now know that the span of the columns of \(A\) is \(\real^n\text{,}\) which tells us that \(A\) is invertible.

    3.1.5.12.

    Answer.
    1. If \(B\xvec = \zerovec\text{,}\) then \(AB\xvec = \xvec = \zerovec\text{.}\)
    2. \(B\) is invertible because \(B\sim I\text{.}\)
    3. Multiply \(AB=I\) on the left by \(B\) and the right by \(C\) and regroup the matrix multiplications.
    Solution.
    1. If \(B\xvec = \zerovec\text{,}\) then \(AB\xvec = A\zerovec = \zerovec = \xvec\text{.}\)
    2. Since the only solution to the homogeneous equation \(B\xvec = \zerovec\) is \(\xvec=\zerovec\text{,}\) we know that the columns of \(B\) are linearly independent and so \(B\) has a pivot position in every column. Since \(B\) is a square matrix, this tells us that \(B\sim I\) so that \(B\) is invertible.
    3. If we multiply \(AB=I\) on the left by \(B\) and the right by \(C\text{,}\) then we have
      \begin{equation*} \begin{aligned} B(AB)C \amp = BIC \\ BA(BC) \amp = BC \\ BAI \amp = I \\ BA \amp = I \end{aligned}. \end{equation*}

    3.2 Bases and coordinate systems
    3.2.5 Exercises

    3.2.5.1.

    Answer.
    1. The vectors are linearly independent and span \(\real^2\text{.}\)
    2. The grid on the figure indicates that
      1. \(\xvec = \twovec{-6}{3}\text{.}\)
      2. \(\xvec = \twovec{-2}{-4}\text{.}\)
      3. \(\xvec = \twovec63\text{.}\)
    3. The grid on the figure indicates that
      1. \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{0}{-1}\text{.}\)
      2. \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{1}{2}\text{.}\)
      3. \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{-2}{-1}\text{.}\)
    4. \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{20}{50}\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The vectors are linearly independent and span \(\real^2\text{.}\) We can see this by forming the matrix
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rr} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} -2 \amp 2 \\ 2 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
    2. The grid on the figure indicates that
      1. \(\xvec = \twovec{-6}{3}\text{.}\)
      2. \(\xvec = \twovec{-2}{-4}\text{.}\)
      3. \(\xvec = \twovec63\text{.}\)
    3. The grid on the figure indicates that
      1. \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{0}{-1}\text{.}\)
      2. \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{1}{2}\text{.}\)
      3. \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{-2}{-1}\text{.}\)
    4. We form the matrix
      \begin{equation*} P_{\bcal}=\left[\begin{array}{rr} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \end{array}\right] = \left[\begin{array}{rr} -2 \amp 2 \\ 2 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      so that \(\xvec=P_{\bcal}\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}\text{.}\) We then need to solve the equation \(P_{\bcal}\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{60}{90}\text{,}\) which gives \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{20}{50}\text{.}\)

    3.2.5.2.

    Answer.
    1. The sets of the vectors are both linearly independent and span \(\real^2\text{.}\)
    2. \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}=\twovec{12/5}{13/5}\) and \(\coords{\xvec}{\ccal}=\twovec{13}{21}\text{.}\)
    3. \(\xvec= \twovec{-2}{16}\) and \(\coords{\xvec}{\ccal}=\twovec{-20}{-38}\text{.}\)
    4. \(\xvec=\twovec{-8}{-13}\) and \(\coords{\xvec}{\ccal}=\twovec{-37/5}{-3/5}\text{.}\)
    5. \(Q = \left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac95 \amp -1 \\ \frac15 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. In both cases, we see that
      \begin{equation*} P_{\bcal}=\left[\begin{array}{rr} \vvec_1\amp\vvec_2 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1\amp0\\ 0\amp1\\ \end{array}\right],~~~ P_{\ccal}=\left[\begin{array}{rr} \wvec_1\amp\wvec_2 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rr} 1\amp0\\ 0\amp1\\ \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      so that both sets of vectors are linearly independent and span \(\real^2\text{.}\)
    2. We solve \(P_{\bcal}\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \xvec\) and \(P_{\ccal}\coords{\xvec}{\ccal} = \xvec\) to find \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}=\twovec{12/5}{13/5}\) and \(\coords{\xvec}{\ccal}=\twovec{13}{21}\text{.}\)
    3. We have \(\xvec=P_{\bcal}\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{-2}{16}\text{.}\) We then solve \(P_{\ccal}\coords{\xvec}{\ccal} = \twovec{-2}{16}\) to find \(\coords{\xvec}{\ccal}=\twovec{-20}{-38}\text{.}\)
    4. In the same way, we find \(\xvec=\twovec{-8}{-13}\) and \(\coords{\xvec}{\ccal}=\twovec{-37/5}{-3/5}\text{.}\)
    5. We have \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = P_{\bcal}^{-1}\xvec = P_{\bcal}^{-1}P_{\ccal}\coords{\xvec}{\ccal}\text{,}\) which shows that \(Q = P_{\bcal}^{-1}P_{\ccal}= \left[\begin{array}{rr} \frac95 \amp -1 \\ \frac15 \amp 0 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)

    3.2.5.3.

    Answer.
    1. The vectors are linearly independent and span \(\real^4\text{.}\)
    2. We have \(\xvec = P_{\bcal}\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}\text{.}\)
    3. We have \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = P_{\bcal}^{-1}\xvec\text{.}\)
    4. \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}=\fourvec{23}{-11}{-2}{9}\text{.}\)
    5. \(\xvec=\fourvec{3}{4}{1}{-3}\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. Form the matrix
      \begin{equation*} P_{\bcal} = \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 1 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 1 \amp 1 \amp 1 \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \end{array}\right]\text{,} \end{equation*}
      which shows that the vectors are linearly independent and span \(\real^4\text{.}\)
    2. We have \(\xvec = P_{\bcal}\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}\text{.}\)
    3. We have \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = P_{\bcal}^{-1}\xvec\) where
      \begin{equation*} P_{\bcal}^{-1} = \left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ -1 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp -1 \amp 1 \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
    4. We find \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}=\fourvec{23}{-11}{-2}{9}\text{.}\)
    5. We find \(\xvec=\fourvec{3}{4}{1}{-3}\text{.}\)

    3.2.5.4.

    Answer.
    1. No, because a basis for \(\real^3\) must contain exactly three vectors.
    2. \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_4\text{.}\)
    3. \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) \(\vvec_4\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_6\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. Looking at the reduced row echelon form, we find
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{rrrrr} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \amp \vvec_3 \amp \vvec_4 \amp \vvec_5 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrrrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp - 2 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \amp 1 \amp -1 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      This shows that the vectors are not linearly independent since there is not a pivot position in every column. Therefore, the set of vectors does not form a basis for \(\real^3\text{.}\) Of course, we also know this because a set of vectors for \(\real^3\) must contain exactly three vectors.
    2. From the reduced row echelon form, we see that the set of vectors spans \(\real^3\) because there is a pivot position in every row. We also see that \(\vvec_3 = -2\vvec_1+\vvec_2\) and \(\vvec_5 = -\vvec_1-\vvec_4\text{.}\) This means that \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_4\) will span \(\real^3\) and therefore form a basis.
    3. In the same way, we see that \(\vvec_1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) \(\vvec_4\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_6\) for a basis for \(\real^4\text{.}\)

    3.2.5.5.

    Answer.
    1. The vectors are linearly independent and span \(\real^3\text{.}\)
    2. \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \threevec{15}{0}{0}\text{.}\)
    3. Since we have \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}=C_{\bcal}^{-1}\xvec\text{,}\) we have \(c_1=\frac13 x_1 + \frac13 x_2 + \frac13 x_3 = \frac13(x_1+x_2+x_3) \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. By forming the matrix \(P_{\bcal}=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \amp \vvec_3 \end{array}\right]\) and finding its reduced row echelon form, we see that the vectors are linearly independent and span \(\real^3\text{.}\) They therefore form a basis of \(\real^3\text{.}\)
    2. We solve \(P_{\bcal}\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \xvec\) to find \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \threevec{15}{0}{0}\text{.}\)
    3. We have
      \begin{equation*} P_{\bcal}= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \amp \frac{1}{2} \\ 1 \amp 0 \amp -1 \\ 1 \amp -\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} \amp \frac{1}{2} \end{array}\right],~~~ P_{\bcal}^{-1}= \left[\begin{array}{rrr} \frac{1}{3} \amp \frac{1}{3} \amp \frac{1}{3} \\ \frac{\sqrt{3}}{3} \amp 0 \amp -\frac{\sqrt{3}}{3} \\ \frac{1}{3} \amp -\frac{2}{3} \amp \frac{1}{3} \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      Since we have \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}=C_{\bcal}^{-1}\xvec\text{,}\) we have \(c_1=\frac13 x_1 + \frac13 x_2 + \frac13 x_3 = \frac13(x_1+x_2+x_3) \text{.}\)

    3.2.5.6.

    Answer.
    1. True
    2. True
    3. True
    4. True
    5. False
    Solution.
    1. True. If the columns of \(A\) form a basis, then \(A\) has a pivot position in every row and every column. Therefore, the reduced row echelon form of \(A\) is the identity matrix, which implies that \(A\) is invertible.
    2. True. The number of vectors in a basis of \(\real^m\) must be \(m\text{.}\)
    3. True. If \(\bcal\) is a basis, then the vectors in \(\bcal\) span \(\real^m\text{,}\) which means that every vector in \(\real^m\) can be written as a linear combination of the vectors in \(\bcal\text{.}\)
    4. True. This is the definition of \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}\text{.}\)
    5. False. The relationship is \(\xvec=P_{\bcal}\coords{\xvec}{\bcal}\text{.}\)

    3.2.5.7.

    Answer.
    1. Yes
    2. Yes
    3. Yes
    4. Yes
    Solution.
    1. Yes. A matrix formed from \(m\) linearly independent vectors in \(\real^m\) will have a pivot position in every column. Since the matrix has the same number of rows and columns, there must also be a pivot position in every row. This means that the vectors span \(\real^m\) and therefore form a basis.
    2. Yes. An invertible matrix is row equivalent to the identity matrix, which means that the columns are linearly independent and span \(\real^m\text{.}\) This implies that the columns form a basis of \(\real^m\text{.}\)
    3. Yes. The matrix \(A\) is row equivalent to the identity matrix so \(B\) must be as well. This means that the columns of \(B\) form a basis for \(\real^m\text{.}\)
    4. Yes. The span of the set of vectors is \(\real^{10}\text{,}\) which says that the associated matrix is square and has a pivot position in every row. Therefore, it must have a pivot position in every column, which means that the set of vectors forms a basis for \(\real^{10}\text{.}\)

    3.2.5.9.

    Answer.
    1. The vectors are linearly independent and span \(\real^2\text{.}\)
    2. \(A\vvec_1 = 3\vvec_1\) and \(A\vvec_2=\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    3. \(\coords{A\vvec_1}{\bcal} = \twovec{3}{0}\) and \(\coords{A\vvec_2}{\bcal} = \twovec{0}{1}\text{.}\)
    4. \(\coords{A\xvec}{\bcal}=\twovec{3}{-5}\text{.}\)
    5. \(D= \left[\begin{array}{rr} 3 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The vectors are linearly independent and span \(\real^2\text{.}\)
    2. We compute that \(A\vvec_1 = 3\vvec_1\) and \(A\vvec_2=\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    3. \(\coords{A\vvec_1}{\bcal} = \twovec{3}{0}\) and \(\coords{A\vvec_2}{\bcal} = \twovec{0}{1}\text{.}\)
    4. We know that \(\xvec = \vvec_1-5\vvec_2\text{,}\) which means that \(A\xvec = 3\vvec_1-5\vvec_2\text{.}\) Therefore, \(\coords{A\xvec}{\bcal}=\twovec{3}{-5}\text{.}\)
    5. If \(\coords{\xvec}{\bcal} = \twovec{c_1}{c_2}\text{,}\) then \(\xvec=c_1\vvec_1+c_2\vvec_2\) and \(A\xvec=3c_1\vvec_1+c_2\vvec_2\text{.}\) Therefore, \(D\twovec{c_1}{c_2} = \twovec{3c_1}{c_2}\text{,}\) which says that \(D= \left[\begin{array}{rr} 3 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \text{.}\)

In the second case, however, we are using only one quarter of the Fourier coefficients. Though the entries in the vector \(\yvec\) differ from those in the original vector \(\xvec\text{,}\) the difference is probably not visually noticeable since the possible values range from 0 to 255.

      \begin{equation*} \yvec=\left[\begin{array}{c} 103 \\ 94 \\ 91 \\ 92 \\ 103 \\ 105 \\ 105 \\ 108 \end{array}\right],~~~ \xvec=\left[\begin{array}{c} 103 \\ 94 \\ 91 \\ 92 \\ 103 \\ 105 \\ 105 \\ 108 \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      In this case, the approximation \(\yvec\) is the same as the original vector \(\xvec\text{.}\)
    3. Now we found the Fourier coefficients to \(\left[\begin{array}{c} 100 \\ -6 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{array}\right]\) and find the approximation \(\yvec\text{,}\) which compares to the original vector \(\xvec\text{.}\)
      \begin{equation*} \yvec=\left[\begin{array}{c} 94 \\ 95 \\ 97 \\ 99 \\ 101 \\ 103 \\ 105 \\ 106 \end{array}\right],~~~ \xvec=\left[\begin{array}{c} 103 \\ 94 \\ 91 \\ 92 \\ 103 \\ 105 \\ 105 \\ 108 \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      Now we see that the approximating vector \(\yvec\) differs from the original vector \(\xvec\text{,}\) though not significantly.
    4. In the first approximation, the reconstructed vector \(\yvec\) agrees with the original vector \(\xvec\text{.}\) In the second approximation, there are only two nonzero Fourier coefficients, and we see that the approximating vector differs from the original vector. In the second case, however, we are using only one quarter of the Fourier coefficients. Though the entries in the vector \(\yvec\) differ from those in the original vector \(\xvec\text{,}\) the difference is probably not visually noticeable since the possible values range from 0 to 255.

    3.3.4.2.

    Answer.
    1. The goal is to represent the image in a format that requires less storage but still allows us to reconstruct the image with most of the visual information preserved.
    2. In the \(YC_bC_r\) color model, the most significant visual information is stored in the \(Y\) channel. Therefore, we should store most of this information. The information in the \(C_b\) and \(C_r\) channels is not as visually significant so we can store a smaller amount of that data without noticing the effect.
    3. As seen in an image, these blocks are relatively small, which means that the approximations we reconstruct are probably not noticeably different from the original. Also, the blocks are small enough so that the image does not typically change a lot over the block. At the same time, the blocks are large enough to allow us to save storage by throwing away some of the Fourier coefficients.
    4. The Discrete Fourier Transform represents the data in a block as its average and variations from the average. Since the blocks are relatively small, there will typically be small variations away from the average. Therefore, we can often ignore those variations without visually detecting the approximation.
    5. Our eyes do not detect individual pixels so we do not detect differences from one pixel to the next. This means that we do not typically notice rapid variations.
    Solution.
    1. The goal is to represent the image in a format that requires less storage but still allows us to reconstruct the image with most of the visual information preserved.
    2. In the \(YC_bC_r\) color model, the most significant visual information is stored in the \(Y\) channel. Therefore, we should store most of this information. The information in the \(C_b\) and \(C_r\) channels is not as visually significant so we can store a smaller amount of that data without noticing the effect.
    3. As seen in an image, these blocks are relatively small, which means that the approximations we reconstruct are probably not noticeably different from the original. Also, the blocks are small enough so that the image does not typically change a lot over the block. At the same time, the blocks are large enough to allow us to save storage by throwing away some of the Fourier coefficients.
    4. The Discrete Fourier Transform represents the data in a block as its average and variations from the average. Since the blocks are relatively small, there will typically be small variations away from the average. Therefore, we can often ignore those variations without visually detecting the approximation.
    5. Our eyes do not detect individual pixels so we do not detect differences from one pixel to the next. This means that we do not typically notice rapid variations.

    3.4 Determinants
    3.4.5 Exercises

    3.4.5.1.

    Answer.
    \(\det(A) = 12\) and \(\det(B) = -24\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    We find that \(\det(A) = 12\) and \(\det(B) = -24\text{.}\)

    3.4.5.2.

    Answer.
    1. \(\det(A) = 1\text{.}\)
    2. \(\det(B) = -1\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The vectors \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\) that form the columns of \(A\) are found by rotating the standard basis vectors \(\evec_1\) and \(\evec_2\text{.}\) Consequently, they are positively oriented and form a \(1\times1\) square. This says that \(\det(A) = 1\text{.}\)
    2. The vectors \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\) that form the columns of \(B\) are found by reflecting the standard basis vectors \(\evec_1\) and \(\evec_2\text{.}\) Consequently, they are negatively oriented and form a \(1\times1\) square. This says that \(\det(B) = -1\text{.}\)

    3.4.5.3.

    Answer.
    1. \(\det(A) = \det(B)\) because \(\det(P) \det(P^{-1}) = 1\text{.}\)
    2. \(\det(A) = 30\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. \(\det(A) = \det(P) \det(B) \det(P^{-1}) = \det(B)\text{.}\)
    2. \(\det(A) = (\det(P)) (2)(-5)(-3) (\det(P^{-1})) = 30\) since \(\det(P) \det(P^{-1}) = 1\text{.}\)

    3.4.5.4.

    Answer.
    1. \(\displaystyle \det(A) = k-16\)
    2. \(k\neq 16\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. We see that \(\det(A) = k-16\text{.}\)
    2. The vectors are linearly independent when the matrix \(A\) is invertible, which means that \(\det(A)\neq 0\text{.}\) Therefore, the vectors are linearly independent when \(k\neq 16\text{.}\)

    3.4.5.5.

    Answer.
    1. False
    2. False
    3. True
    4. True
    5. False
    Solution.
    1. False. This is a row replacement operation, which leaves the determinant unchanged.
    2. False. Applying an interchange operation changes the sign of the determinant.
    3. True. Scaling a row of \(A\) by \(r\) multiplies the determinant by \(r\text{.}\)
    4. True. Row operations either leave the determinant unchanged, change its sign, or multiply it by a nonzero number. Therefore, if \(\det(A')=0\) and \(A\) and \(A'\) are related through a sequence of row operations, then \(\det(A) = 0\text{.}\)
    5. False. It is true that \(\det(I) = 1\text{,}\) but a sequence of row operations that cause \(A\) and \(I\) to be row equivalent may multiply the determinant by a nonzero number or change its sign. We do know, however, that \(\det(A)\neq 0\) and so \(A\) is invertible.

    3.4.5.6.

    Answer.
    1. \(\det(2A) = -64\text{.}\)
    2. \(\det(A^3) = -8\text{.}\)
    3. \(\det(AB) = -10\text{.}\)
    4. \(\det(-A) = 2\text{.}\)
    5. \(\det (AB^{-1}) = -\frac 25\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. Multiplying the entire matrix by \(2\) scales each row by \(2\text{.}\) Therefore, \(\det(2A) = 2^5\det(A) = -64\text{.}\)
    2. We have \(\det(A^3) = (\det(A))^3 = -8\text{.}\)
    3. We know that \(\det(AB) = \det(A) \det(B) = -10\text{.}\)
    4. Multiplying the matrix by \(-1\) scales each row by \(-1\) so we have \(\det(-A) = (-1)^5\det(A) = 2\text{.}\)
    5. \(\det(AB^{-1}) = \det(A) \det(B^{-1}) = \det(A)/\det(B) = -\frac 25\text{.}\)

    3.4.5.7.

    Answer.
    1. If \(A\) and \(B\) are invertible, then \(\det(A) \neq 0\) and \(\det(B) \neq 0\text{.}\) Therefore, \(\det(AB) = \det(A)\det(B) \neq 0\text{,}\) which shows that \(AB\) is invertible.
    2. If \(AB\) is invertible, then \(\det (AB) = \det(A) \det(B) \neq 0\text{.}\) This implies that \(\det(A)\neq0\) and \(\det(B) \neq 0\text{,}\) from which we conclude that both \(A\) and \(B\) are invertible.
    Solution.
    1. If \(A\) and \(B\) are invertible, then \(\det(A) \neq 0\) and \(\det(B) \neq 0\text{.}\) Therefore, \(\det(AB) = \det(A)\det(B) \neq 0\text{,}\) which shows that \(AB\) is invertible.
    2. If \(AB\) is invertible, then \(\det (AB) = \det(A) \det(B) \neq 0\text{.}\) This implies that \(\det(A)\neq0\) and \(\det(B) \neq 0\text{,}\) from which we conclude that both \(A\) and \(B\) are invertible.

    3.4.5.8.

    Answer.
    In all four cases, the determinant must be zero.
    Solution.
    1. The determinant must be zero. This is because the matrix cannot be invertible since there is a row without a pivot. Also, applying a cofactor expansion along that row will produce zero for the determinant.
    2. The determinant is zero again. If we multiply one of the rows by \(-1\) and add it to the other row, we obtain a row whose entries are all zero. This operation does not change the determinant, but we know that the determinant of the new matrix is zero by the previous part of this problem.
    3. The determinant must be zero. This is because the columns are not linearly independent, which means that the matrix cannot be invertible. Hence, the determinant is zero.
    4. The determinant must be zero. This is because the columns are not linearly independent, which means that the matrix cannot be invertible. Hence, the determinant is zero.

    3.4.5.9.

    Answer.
    1. \(\det(A) = 2x-y-2z = 0\text{.}\)
    2. If we replace the third column by either \(\vvec_1\) or \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) we obtain a matrix whose columns are linearly dependent.
    3. If \(\det(A)=0\) for a vector \(\threevec xyz\text{,}\) then \(\threevec xyz\) is a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. Using a cofactor expansion, we have \(\det(A) = 2x-y-2z = 0\text{.}\)
    2. If we replace the third column by either \(\vvec_1\) or \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) we obtain a matrix whose columns are linearly dependent. Therefore, the determinant of the matrix must be zero.
    3. If \(\det(A) = 0\) for some vector \(\threevec xyz\text{,}\) then the columns of \(A\) must be linear dependent. Therefore, \(\threevec xyz\) is a linear combination of \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\) and lies in the plane that is their span.

    3.4.5.10.

    Answer.
    1. Column replacements do not change the determinant because \(\det(R)=1\text{.}\)
    2. Scaling a column by \(r\) multiplies the determinant by \(r\) because \(\det(S)=r\text{.}\)
    3. Interchanges change the sign of the determinant because \(\det(P)=-1\text{.}\)
    4. \(\det(A) = -12\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The columns of \(AR\) are found by multiplying the columns of \(R\) by \(A\text{.}\) If we remember that a vector multiplied by a matrix forms a linear combination of the columns of \(A\text{,}\) we see that the first column of \(AR\) is \(\vvec_1-3\vvec_2\) and that the other two columns are unchanged.
      Since \(\det(R) = 1\text{,}\) we have \(\det(AR) = \det(A)\text{,}\) which says that this operation does not change the determinant.
    2. Multiplying by \(S\) multiplies the determinant by \(3\) since \(\det(S)=3\text{.}\)
    3. Multiplying by \(P\) changes the sign of the determinant since \(\det(P) = -1\text{.}\)
    4. If we interchange the first and third columns of \(A\text{,}\) we have
      \begin{equation*} A_1=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp -3 \amp 0 \\ 4 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      and \(\det(A) = -\det(A_1)\text{.}\) Now we will multiply the first column by \(3\) and add to the second column to obtain
      \begin{equation*} A_2=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 4 \amp 13 \amp 1 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      and \(\det(A) = -\det(A_2)\text{.}\) Let’s interchange the second and third columns so that
      \begin{equation*} A_3=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 4 \amp 1 \amp 13 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ \end{array}\right] \end{equation*}
      so that \(\det(A) = \det(A_3)\) and then multiply the second column by \(-13\) and add to the third column so that
      \begin{equation*} A_4=\left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 4 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp -12 \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      This gives \(\det(A) = \det(A_4) = -12\text{.}\)

    3.4.5.11.

    Answer.
    \(\det(A) = -1\text{,}\) \(\det(B) = 1\text{,}\) and \(\det(C) = abcd\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    It takes three interchanges to see that \(A\sim I\text{,}\) which implies that \(\det(A) = -1\text{.}\)
    Two interchanges show us that \(A\sim I\) and hence \(\det(B) = 1\text{.}\)
    Two interchanges produce a diagonal matrix so that \(\det(C) = abcd\text{.}\)

    3.4.5.12.

    Answer.
    1. \(\det(A) = -1\text{,}\) \(\det(B) = 0\text{,}\) \(\det(C) = 1\text{,}\) and \(\det(D) = 0\text{.}\)
    2. If \(E\) and \(F\) are the \(6\times6\) and \(7\times7\) matrices, respectively, we expect that \(\det(E) = -1\) and \(\det(F) = 0\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. \(\det(A) = -1\text{,}\) \(\det(B) = 0\text{,}\) \(\det(C) = 1\text{,}\) and \(\det(D) = 0\text{.}\)
    2. If \(E\) and \(F\) are the \(6\times6\) and \(7\times7\) matrices, respectively, we expect that \(\det(E) = -1\) and \(\det(F) = 0\text{.}\)

    3.4.5.13.

    Answer.
    1. Perform a sequence of row operations to form an upper triangular matrix.
    2. If \(a\text{,}\) \(b\text{,}\) and \(c\) are distinct, then \(\det(V) \neq 0\text{.}\)
    3. The determinant is \((d-a)(d-b)(d-c)(c-a)(c-b)(b-a)\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. If \(a\text{,}\) \(b\text{,}\) and \(c\) are not distinct, we see that \(\det(V)=0\) because two of the rows are identical. Therefore, we apply the following row operations, assuming \(a\text{,}\) \(b\text{,}\) and \(c\) are distinct:
      \begin{equation*} \begin{array}{rl} V = \amp \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp a \amp a^2 \\ 1 \amp b \amp b^2 \\ 1 \amp c \amp c^2 \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp a \amp a^2 \\ 0 \amp b-a \amp b^2-a^2 \\ 0 \amp c-a \amp c^2-a^2 \\ \end{array}\right] \\ \sim \amp \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp a \amp a^2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp b+a \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp c+a \\ \end{array}\right] \sim \left[\begin{array}{rrr} 1 \amp a \amp a^2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp b+a \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp c-b \\ \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{array} \end{equation*}
      Because of the scalings by \(b-a\) and \(c-a\text{,}\) we have \(\det(V) = (b-a)(c-a)(c-b)\text{.}\)
    2. If \(a\text{,}\) \(b\text{,}\) and \(c\) are distinct, then \(\det(V) \neq 0\text{,}\) which implies that \(V\) is invertible.
    3. In the \(4\times4\) case, we have
      \begin{equation*} \det\left[\begin{array}{rrrr} 1 \amp a \amp a^2 \amp a^3 \\ 1 \amp b \amp b^2 \amp b^3 \\ 1 \amp c \amp c^2 \amp c^3 \\ 1 \amp d \amp d^2 \amp d^3 \\ \end{array}\right] = (d-a)(d-b)(d-c)(c-a)(c-b)(b-a)\text{.} \end{equation*}

    3.5 Subspaces
    3.5.5 Exercises

    3.5.5.1.

    Answer.
    1. \(\nul(A)\) is a subspace of \(\real^6\) and \(\col(A)\) is a subspace of \(\real^4\text{.}\)
    2. \(\dim~\nul(A) = 3\) and \(\dim~\col(A)=3\text{.}\)
    3. \(\displaystyle \fourvec{2}{-4}{6}{4}, \fourvec{0}{-1}{0}{-1}, \fourvec{0}{0}{3}{0}\)
    4. \(\displaystyle \left[\begin{array}{r} 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ \end{array}\right], \left[\begin{array}{r} 0 \\ 2 \\ -1 \\ 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ \end{array}\right], \left[\begin{array}{r} 0 \\ -3 \\ -4 \\ 0 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{array}\right]\)
    Solution.
    1. \(\nul(A)\) is a subspace of \(\real^6\) and \(\col(A)\) is a subspace of \(\real^4\text{.}\)
    2. Because there are three pivot positions, we see that \(\rank(A) = 3\text{.}\) Therefore, \(\dim~\nul(A) = 6-3=3\) and \(\dim~\col(A)=3\text{.}\)
    3. A basis for \(\col(A)\) is given by the columns of \(A\) that contain pivot positions. Therefore, a basis is
      \begin{equation*} \fourvec{2}{-4}{6}{4}, \fourvec{0}{-1}{0}{-1}, \fourvec{0}{0}{3}{0}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    4. We can write a parametric description for the solution space to the homogeneous equation as
      \begin{equation*} \xvec = \left[\begin{array}{r} x_1 \\ 2x_4 - 3x_6 \\ -x_4-4x_6 \\ x_4 \\ x_6 \\ x_6 \end{array}\right] = x_1 \left[\begin{array}{r} 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ \end{array}\right] +x_4 \left[\begin{array}{r} 0 \\ 2 \\ -1 \\ 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ \end{array}\right] + x_6 \left[\begin{array}{r} 0 \\ -3 \\ -4 \\ 0 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}
      Therefore, a basis for \(\nul(A)\) is
      \begin{equation*} \left[\begin{array}{r} 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ \end{array}\right], \left[\begin{array}{r} 0 \\ 2 \\ -1 \\ 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ \end{array}\right], \left[\begin{array}{r} 0 \\ -3 \\ -4 \\ 0 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{array}\right]\text{.} \end{equation*}

    Answer.
    1. Yes
    2. No
    3. No
    4. Yes
    5. No
