    2. \(\displaystyle q(\xvec) = x^2 + 4xy + y^2\)
    3. \(q(\vvec) = \vvec\cdot(A\vvec) = \lambda\vvec\cdot\vvec = \lambda\text{.}\)

    6.1.4.11.

    Answer.
    1. \(y=3-x/2\text{.}\)
    2. \(\xvec-\pvec\) is orthogonal to \(\nvec\text{.}\)
    3. \(\displaystyle 0\)
    4. Apply the distributive property
    5. \(\displaystyle x+2y = 6\)
    6. \(\nvec=\twovec{A}{B}\) is perpendicular to the line.
    Solution.
    1. The slope \(m=-1/2\) and the intercept \(b=3\) so \(y=3-x/2\text{.}\)
    2. The vector \(\xvec-\pvec\) is in the direction of the line so it is orthogonal to \(\nvec\text{.}\)
    3. Since these vectors are orthogonal, their dot product is \(0\text{.}\)
    4. Since \(\nvec\cdot(\xvec-\pvec) = 0\text{,}\) we have \(\nvec\cdot\xvec - \nvec\cdot\pvec = 0\text{,}\) which implies that \(\nvec\cdot\xvec = \nvec\cdot\pvec\text{.}\)
    5. \(\pvec=\twovec22\) and \(\nvec=\twovec12\text{.}\) This gives \(x+2y = 6\text{.}\) We can rearrange this to have the form \(y=3-x/2\text{.}\)
    6. If we choose a vector \(\nvec=\twovec{A}{B}\) that is perpendicular to the line and a point \(\pvec\) on the line, we have \(\nvec\cdot\xvec = Ax + By = \nvec\cdot\pvec = C\text{.}\)

The reduced row echelon form shows that the third column is a linear combination of the first two so \(\threevec{-1}12\) and \(\threevec{-2}31\) form a basis for \(\col(A)\text{.}\)

      1. Yes
      2. No
      3. No
      4. Yes
    Solution.

      2. \((BAB^{-1})^T = (B^{-1})^TA^TB^T\) so this matrix need not be symmetric.
      3. \((AA^T)^T = (A^T)^TA^T = AA^T\) so this matrix is symmetric.

    6.2.5.7.

    Answer.
    1. Use properties of the matrix transpose.
    2. Because they have the same characteristic polynomial.
    3. \(\displaystyle A^T = (P^T)^{-1} D P^T\)
    Solution.

    2. The eigenvalues of a matrix are given by the roots of its characteristic polynomial. Since \(A\) and \(A^T\) have the same characteristic polynomial, they have the same eigenvalues.

    6.2.5.9.

    Answer.
    1. Use properties of the transpose.
    2. Compute the dot product of \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    3. It follows that \((\lambda_1-\lambda_2)\vvec_1\cdot\vvec_2 = 0\)
    Solution.
    1. \(\xvec\cdot(A\yvec) = \xvec^TA\yvec = (A^T\xvec)^T\yvec = (A^T\xvec)\cdot\yvec\text{.}\)
    2. \(A^T = A\) and \(\vvec_1\cdot\vvec_2=0\text{.}\)
    3. \begin{align*} \vvec_1\cdot(A\vvec_2) \amp {}={} (A\vvec_1)\cdot\vvec_2\\ \lambda_2\vvec_1\cdot\vvec_2 \amp {}={} \lambda_1\vvec_1\cdot\vvec_2\\ (\lambda_1-\lambda_2)\vvec_1\cdot\vvec_2\amp{}={} 0 \end{align*}
      so \(\vvec_1\cdot\vvec_2=0\) since \(\lambda_1-\lambda_2\neq 0\text{.}\)

    3. \(\rank(A) = 3\) so \(\rank(A^T)=\dim\col(A^T) = \dim\row(A) = 3\text{.}\) A basis for \(\row(A)\) are the three rows of \(A\text{.}\) \(\row(A)^\perp = \nul(A)\) is one-dimensional with basis \(\fourvec{-2}100\text{.}\)

    6.3.4.1.

    Answer.
    1. \(\wvec_1\cdot\wvec_2=0\text{.}\)
    2. \(\bhat = \threevec{1/2}1{1/2}\text{.}\)
    3. \(\uvec_1=\frac{1}{\len{\wvec_1}}\wvec_1 = \threevec{1/\sqrt{3}}{1/\sqrt{3}}{1/\sqrt{3}}\) and \(\uvec_2=\frac{1}{\len{\wvec_2}}\wvec_2 = \threevec{1/\sqrt{6}}{-2/\sqrt{6}}{1/\sqrt{6}}\)
    4. \(\displaystyle P=\begin{bmatrix} 1/2 \amp 0 \amp 1/2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 1/2 \amp 0 \amp 1/2 \end{bmatrix}\)
    5. \(\displaystyle \rank(P) = 2\)
    Solution.
    1. Check that \(\wvec_1\cdot\wvec_2=0\text{.}\)
    2. Applying the Projection Formula gives \(\bhat = \threevec{1/2}1{1/2}\text{.}\)
    3. \(\uvec_1=\frac{1}{\len{\wvec_1}}\wvec_1 = \threevec{1/\sqrt{3}}{1/\sqrt{3}}{1/\sqrt{3}}\) and \(\uvec_2=\frac{1}{\len{\wvec_2}}\wvec_2 = \threevec{1/\sqrt{6}}{-2/\sqrt{6}}{1/\sqrt{6}}\)
    4. Form \(Q=\begin{bmatrix}\uvec_1 \amp \uvec_2 \end{bmatrix}\) so that \(P=QQ^T=\begin{bmatrix} 1/2 \amp 0 \amp 1/2 \\ 0 \amp 1 \amp 0 \\ 1/2 \amp 0 \amp 1/2 \end{bmatrix}\)
    5. Since \(\col(P)=W\text{,}\) we have \(\rank(P) = 2\)

    6.3.4.2.

    Answer.
    1. \(\wvec_1\cdot\wvec_1=0\text{,}\) \(\wvec_1\cdot\wvec_3=0\text{,}\) and \(\wvec_2\cdot\wvec_3=0\text{.}\)
    2. \(\displaystyle A^TA = \begin{bmatrix} 3 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 6 \end{bmatrix}\)
    3. \(\bvec=-2\wvec_1+3\wvec_2+2\wvec_3\text{.}\)
    4. \begin{equation*} \uvec_1=\threevec{1/\sqrt{3}}{1/\sqrt{3}} {1/\sqrt{3}},\hspace{24pt} \uvec_2=\threevec{-1/\sqrt{2}}0{1/\sqrt{2}}, \hspace{24pt} \uvec_3=\threevec{1/\sqrt{6}}{-2/\sqrt{6}} {1/\sqrt{6}},\hspace{24pt} \end{equation*}
    5. \(\displaystyle QQ^T=I\)
    Solution.
    1. Check that all three dot products \(\wvec_1\cdot\wvec_1=0\text{,}\) \(\wvec_1\cdot\wvec_3=0\text{,}\) and \(\wvec_2\cdot\wvec_3=0\text{.}\)
    2. \(A^TA = \begin{bmatrix} 3 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 6 \end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix} \wvec_1\cdot\wvec_1\amp \wvec_1\cdot\wvec_2\amp \wvec_1\cdot\wvec_3 \\ \wvec_2\cdot\wvec_1\amp \wvec_2\cdot\wvec_2\amp \wvec_2\cdot\wvec_3 \\ \wvec_3\cdot\wvec_1\amp \wvec_3\cdot\wvec_2\amp \wvec_3\cdot\wvec_3 \\ \end{bmatrix} \text{.}\) This matrix is diagonal because the vectors form an orthogonal set.
    3. We may find the weights of this linear combination by finding \(c_i=\frac{b\cdot\wvec_i}{\wvec_i\cdot\wvec_i}\) so that \(\bvec=-2\wvec_1+3\wvec_2+2\wvec_3\text{.}\)
    4. \begin{equation*} \uvec_1=\threevec{1/\sqrt{3}}{1/\sqrt{3}} {1/\sqrt{3}},\hspace{24pt} \uvec_2=\threevec{-1/\sqrt{2}}0{1/\sqrt{2}}, \hspace{24pt} \uvec_3=\threevec{1/\sqrt{6}}{-2/\sqrt{6}} {1/\sqrt{6}},\hspace{24pt} \end{equation*}
    5. The columns of \(Q\) form an orthonormal basis for \(\real^3\) so \(Q\) is orthogonal. Therefore, \(QQ^T=I\text{.}\)

    6.3.4.8.

    Answer.
    1. \(Q\xvec\cdot(Q\yvec) = (Q\xvec)^T\cdot(Q\yvec) = \xvec^TQ^TQ\yvec = \xvec^T\yvec=\xvec\cdot\yvec\text{.}\)
    2. \(\displaystyle \len{Q\xvec}^2 = (Q\xvec)\cdot(Q\xvec) = \xvec\cdot\xvec = \len{\xvec}^2\)
    3. If \(Q\vvec = \lambda\vvec\text{,}\) then \(\len{Q\vvec} = |\lambda|\len{\vvec}=\len{\vvec}\) so \(|\lambda|=1\)
    Solution.
    1. \(Q\xvec\cdot(Q\yvec) = (Q\xvec)^T\cdot(Q\yvec) = \xvec^TQ^TQ\yvec = \xvec^T\yvec=\xvec\cdot\yvec\text{.}\)
    2. \(\displaystyle \len{Q\xvec}^2 = (Q\xvec)\cdot(Q\xvec) = \xvec\cdot\xvec = \len{\xvec}^2\)
    3. If \(Q\vvec = \lambda\vvec\text{,}\) then \(\len{Q\vvec} = |\lambda|\len{\vvec}=\len{\vvec}\) so \(|\lambda|=1\)

    6.3.4.9.

    Answer.
    1. Since \(Q^TQ=I\text{,}\) we have \(\det(Q^TQ)=\det(I)=1\text{.}\)
    2. The four columns of \(Q\) form a basis for the column space \(\col(QQ^T)\)
    3. If \(\bvec^\perp=\bvec-\bhat\text{,}\) then \(\bvec-\bperp = \bhat\) is in \(W\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. Since \(Q^TQ=I\text{,}\) we have \(\det(Q^TQ)=\det(Q^T)\det(Q)=(\det(Q))^2 = \det(I) = 1\)
    2. The columns of \(Q\) form an orthonormal basis for \(W\text{,}\) a 4-dimensional subspace of \(\real^8\text{.}\) Therefore, \(QQ^T\) projects vectors orthogonally onto \(W\) so \(\col(QQ^T)=W\) and \(\rank(QQ^T)=4\text{.}\)
    3. If \(\bvec^\perp=\bvec-\bhat\text{,}\) then \(\bvec-\bperp = \bhat\) is in \(W\text{,}\) the orthogonal complement of \(W^\perp\text{.}\) This means that \(\bperp\) is the orthogonal projection of \(\bvec\) onto \(W^\perp\text{.}\)

    6.3.4.10.

    Answer.
    1. \(Q^TQ=I\text{.}\)
    2. \(Q^TQ=I\text{.}\)
    3. \(\uvec_2=\twovec{-\sin\theta}{\cos\theta}\) or \(\twovec{\sin\theta}{-\cos\theta}\)
    4. The first column of \(Q\) has the form \(\uvec_1=\twovec{\cos\theta}{\sin\theta}\text{.}\) Now apply the result of the last part of this problem.
    Solution.
    1. If this matrix is \(Q\text{,}\) we have \(Q^TQ=I\text{.}\)
    2. If this matrix is \(Q\text{,}\) we have \(Q^TQ=I\text{.}\)
    3. \(\uvec_2=\twovec{-\sin\theta}{\cos\theta}\) or \(\twovec{\sin\theta}{-\cos\theta}\)
    4. If \(Q=\begin{bmatrix}\uvec_1\amp\uvec_2\end{bmatrix}\) is an orthogonal \(2\times2\) matrix, then \(\uvec_1\) is a unit vector and has the form \(\uvec_1=\twovec{\cos\theta}{\sin\theta}\) for some angle \(\theta\text{.}\) By the last part of this problem, there are only two choices for \(\uvec_2\text{,}\) one of which gives a rotation and one of which gives a reflection.

    Answer.
    \(Q=\begin{bmatrix} 2/3 \amp 1/\sqrt{5} \\ -1/3 \amp 2/\sqrt{5} \\ 2/3 \amp 0 \end{bmatrix}\) and \(R = \begin{bmatrix} 6 \amp 6 \\ 0 \amp 3\sqrt{5} \end{bmatrix}\)
    Solution.
    Applying Gram-Schmidt, we have \(\wvec_1=\threevec4{-2}4\) and \(\wvec_2=\threevec360\text{,}\) which leads to \(\uvec_1=\threevec{2/3}{-1/3}{2/3}\) and \(\uvec_2=\threevec{1/\sqrt{5}}{2/\sqrt{5}}0\text{.}\) It therefore follows that \(\vvec_1 = 6\uvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2 = 6\uvec_1 + 3\sqrt{5}\uvec_2\text{.}\)
    This leads to \(A=QR\) where \(Q=\begin{bmatrix} 2/3 \amp 1/\sqrt{5} \\ -1/3 \amp 2/\sqrt{5} \\ 2/3 \amp 0 \end{bmatrix}\) and \(R = \begin{bmatrix} 6 \amp 6 \\ 0 \amp 3\sqrt{5} \end{bmatrix} \text{.}\)

    6.4.4.3.

    Answer.

      1. \(\yvec = Q^T\bvec\text{.}\)

      1. Since \(Q^TQ = I\text{,}\) we have \(\yvec = Q^T\bvec\text{.}\)
      2. \(R\) is upper triangular so this equation can be solved using back substitution.

    2. Let \(Q\) be the matrix whose columns are the orthonormal basis that results from Gram-Schmidt. Then
      \begin{equation*} QQ^T = \begin{bmatrix} 7/10 \amp -1/5 \amp 3/10 \amp 1/5 \amp 1/5 \\ -1/5 \amp 1/5 \amp 1/5 \amp -1/5\amp -1/5 \\ 3/10 \amp 1/5 \amp 7/10 \amp -1/5 \amp -1/5 \\ 1/5 \amp -1/5 \amp -1/5 \amp 7/10 \amp -3/10 \\ 1/5 \amp -1/5 \amp -1/5 \amp -3/10 \amp 7/10 \\ \end{bmatrix} \end{equation*}

    Answer.
    1. We find \(\wvec=\zerovec\text{.}\)
    2. This set of vectors is linearly dependent.
    Solution.
    1. We find
      \begin{equation*} \wvec_1 = \threevec211,\hspace{24pt} \wvec_2 = \threevec{-1}11,\hspace{24pt} \wvec_3 = \threevec000 \end{equation*}
      Since \(\wvec_3=\zerovec\text{,}\) this does not produce a basis for \(\real^3\text{.}\)
    2. The vector \(\vvec_3=2\vvec_1-\vvec_2\) so this set of vectors is linearly dependent. Since \(\vvec_3\) is in the span of \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{,}\) projecting \(\vvec_3\) into that subspace gives \(\widehat{\vvec}_3 = \vvec_3\) so that \(\wvec_3 = \vvec_3 - \widehat{\vvec}_3 = \zerovec\text{.}\)

    6.4.4.6.

    Answer.
    1. True
    2. True
    3. False
    4. True
    5. True
    Solution.
    1. True. Since \(Q^TQ=I\text{,}\) we have \(Q^TA = Q^TQR = R\text{.}\)
    2. True. Since \(R\) is upper triangular and the diagonal entries of \(R\) are the lengths of the nonzero vectors \(\wvec_j\text{,}\) we have \(\det(R)\neq 0\text{,}\) which means that \(R\) is invertible.
    3. False, because \(Q^TQ=I\text{.}\)
    4. True. In fact, they are an orthonormal basis for \(\col(A)\text{.}\)
    5. True. If \(Q^T\xvec = \zerovec\text{,}\) then \(\uvec_i\cdot\xvec=0\) for every vector in an orthonormal basis of \(\col(A)\text{.}\) Therefore, \(\xvec\) is orthogonal to \(\col(A)\text{.}\)

    6.4.4.7.

    Answer.
    1. \(\displaystyle 7\times7\)
    2. \(\displaystyle 4\times4\)
    3. \(\displaystyle 4\times4\)
    4. They form an orthogonal set.
    Solution.
    1. \(QQ^T\) is \(7\times7\) and projects vectors orthogonally onto \(\col(A)\text{.}\)
    2. \(Q^TQ\) is the \(4\times4\) identity matrix because the product computes dot products between the columns of \(Q\text{.}\)
    3. \(R\) is a \(4\times4\) upper triangular matrix.
    4. The columns of \(A\) form an orthogonal set.

    6.4.4.8.

    Answer.
    1. \(\displaystyle \avec_i\cdot\avec_j\)
    2. \(\displaystyle \rvec_i\cdot\rvec_j\)
    3. \(\displaystyle A^TA = (QR)^T(QR) = R^TR\)
    4. This follows from the previous parts of this exercise.
    Solution.
    1. The entries of \(A^TA\) are the dot products \(\avec_i\cdot\avec_j\text{.}\)
    2. The entries of \(R^TR\) are the dot products \(\rvec_i\cdot\rvec_j\text{.}\)
    3. \(\displaystyle A^TA = (QR)^T(QR) = R^TQ^TQR = R^TIR = R^TR\)
    4. This follows from the previous parts of this exercise.

    6.5.6.4.

    Answer.
    1. \(\displaystyle \begin{bmatrix} 1 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 1 \amp 2 \\ 1 \amp 2 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 2 \amp 2 \\ 1 \amp 3 \amp 2 \\ 1 \amp 3 \amp 3 \\ \end{bmatrix} \xvec = \begin{bmatrix} 4.2 \\ 3.3 \\ 5.9 \\ 5.1 \\ 7.5 \\ 6.3 \end{bmatrix}\)

    6.5.6.5.

    Answer.
    1. True
    2. True
    3. False
    4. True
    5. False
    Solution.
    1. True. If \(A\xvec=\bvec\text{,}\) then \(\bvec\) is in \(\col(A)\) so \(A\xhat = \bhat = \bvec\text{.}\)
    2. True. If \(R^2 = 1 - \frac{|A\xhat - \bvec|^2} {|\widetilde{\bvec}|^2} = 1\text{,}\) then \(|A\xhat-\bvec| = 0\text{.}\) Therefore, \(A\xhat = \bvec\text{.}\)
    3. False. The product \(Q^TQ = I\) rather than the matrix that projects vectors orthogonally onto \(\col(A)\text{.}\)
    4. True, numerical issues are more likely to arise when solving the normal equations.
    5. False. The normal equations \(A^TA\xhat = A^T\bvec\) gives the least-squares approximate solution.

    6.5.6.6.

    Answer.
    1. \(\bvec\) is in \(\col(A)^\perp = \nul(A^T)\text{.}\)
    2. \(\xhat = A^T\bvec\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. Since \(A\xhat = \bhat\) and \(\xhat = \zerovec\text{,}\) we know that \(\bhat=\zerovec\text{.}\) This says that \(\bvec\) is in \(\col(A)^\perp = \nul(A^T)\text{.}\)
    2. In this case, \(\bhat = AA^T\bvec\text{,}\) which means we have \(A\xhat = AA^T\bvec\text{.}\) If we multiply both sides by \(A^T\text{,}\) we have \(\xhat = A^T\bvec\text{.}\)

    6.5.6.7.

    Answer.
    1. The matrix \(A\) will be the \(27\times 2\) matrix whose first column is all 1’s and whose second column is the vector of years. The vector \(\bvec\) is the vector that records the number of people.

    Answer.

    1. The matrix \(A\) is the \(9\times2\) matrix whose first column is all 1’s and whose second column is the vector of the logarithms of the semi-major axes. The vector \(\bvec\) is the vector that records the logarithms of the periods.
    2. We find \(\xhat=\twovec{\ln(C)}{r} = \twovec{-0.0036}{1.501}\) so that \(C=e^{-0.0036} = 0.996\) and \(r=1.50\text{.}\) This means that \(P = 0.996L^{1.5}\text{.}\)
    3. \(R^2=1.000\text{,}\) which means that we have a very good fit because this data reflects a physical law.
    4. \(P = 0.996(4.0)^{1.5}=7.99\) years.
    5. The expression \(\ln(P) = \ln(C) + r\ln(L)\) means that \(\ln(76) = \ln(C) + r\ln(L)\text{.}\) Solving for \(L = 17.9\) AU.

    Solution.
    1. The matrix \(A\) will be the Vandermonde matrix of degree \(k\) using the altitude readings while \(\bvec\) is the vector of temperatures.
    2. \(k=6\) appears to give a good fit without overfitting.
    3. We have
      \begin{equation*} 18.0-10.3x+0.35x^2+0.0003x^3-0.0001x^4+1.5\times10^{-6}x^5 -4.7\times 10^{-9}x^6\text{.} \end{equation*}
    4. A small value of \(k\) does not adequately track important features of the data. If we increase \(k\) too far, the graph tries too hard to match the data, and we see spurious features in the graph.
    5. \(k=6\) seems to work well. With this function, we estimate the temperature to be \(-7\) degrees Celsius.

    2. Increasing the living area or the lot size will cause the house price to increase, but an older house will cost less.

    2. \(\beta_1\) and \(\beta_2\) are positive, which means that increasing the living area or the lot size will cause the house price to increase. However, \(\beta_3\) is negative, which means that an older house will cost less.
    3. Because \(\beta_3=-263.6\text{,}\) we would expect a house that’s one year older to cost \(\$263.60\) less.
    4. \(R^2=0.59\text{,}\) which is far from perfect without being terrible. There are other factors that are not included in the dataset that can also influence the house price, such as number of bedrooms and the location.
    5. We estimate \(\$177966\text{.}\)

    6.5.6.11.

    Answer.
    1. Use the fact that \(\xvec\cdot(A^TA\xvec)= (A\xvec)^T(A\xvec)\text{.}\)
    2. It must be true that \(\xvec=\zerovec\text{.}\)
    3. This follows from the previous two parts of this exercise.
    4. The columns of \(A^TA\) must be linearly independent.
    Solution.
    1. Starting with the assumption that \(A^TA\xvec = \zerovec\text{,}\) we dot both sides of the equation with \(\xvec\) to obtain \(\xvec \cdot A^TA\xvec = 0\text{.}\) From here, we write
      \begin{equation*} \begin{aligned} \xvec\cdot A^TA\xvec \amp = 0 \\ \xvec^TA^TA\xvec \amp = 0 \\ (A\xvec)^T(A\xvec) \amp = 0 \\ (A\xvec)\cdot (A\xvec) \amp = 0 \\ \len{A\xvec}^2 \amp = 0. \\ \end{aligned} \end{equation*}
      This says that the length of \(A\xvec\) must be zero, which tells us that \(A\xvec=\zerovec\text{.}\)
    2. If the columns of \(A\) are linearly independent, we know that the only solution to the homogeneous equation \(A\xvec = \zerovec\) is \(\xvec = \zerovec\text{.}\)
    3. The previous two parts of this exercise tell us that \(A^TA\xvec = \zerovec\) implies that \(A\xvec = \zerovec\text{,}\) which implies that \(\xvec = \zerovec\text{.}\)
    4. Since the only solution to the homogeneous equation \(A^TA\xvec = \zerovec\) is \(\xvec=\zerovec\text{,}\) the columns of \(A^TA\) are linearly independent, which means that \(A^TA\xhat=A^T\bvec\) has only one solution.

    6.5.6.12.

    Answer.
    1. \(\bvec\cdot\onevec\) simply sums the components of \(\bvec\text{.}\)
    2. \(\onevec\) is a column of \(A\text{.}\)
    3. \(\bperp\) is in \(\col(A)^\perp\text{.}\)
    4. Use the fact that \(\bhat\) and \(\bperp\) are orthogonal.
    5. This follows from \(\bvec = \bhat + \bperp\text{.}\)
    6. This follow from \(\var(\bvec) = \var(\bhat) + \var(\bperp)\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. \(\bvec\cdot\onevec\) simply sums the components of \(\bvec\text{.}\)
    2. The examples we’ve seen fit the data using functions that have a constant term such as \(y=\beta_0+\beta_1x\text{.}\) This means that \(\onevec\) will be a column of the matrix \(A\) and hence in \(\col(A)\text{.}\)
    3. Since \(\onevec\) is in \(\col(A)\text{,}\) any vector in \(\col(A)^\perp\text{,}\) such as \(\bperp\text{,}\) will be orthogonal to \(\onevec\text{.}\)
    4. Notice that
      \begin{equation*} \bvec\cdot\onevec = \bhat\cdot\onevec + \bperp\cdot\onevec = \bhat\cdot\onevec \end{equation*}
      which says that
      \begin{equation*} \widetilde{\bvec} = \widetilde{\bhat} + \bperp\text{.} \end{equation*}
      Since \(\bhat\) and \(\bperp\) are orthogonal, we have \(\widetilde{\bhat}\cdot\bperp = 0\text{.}\) This means that
      \begin{equation*} |\widetilde{\bvec}|^2 = (\widetilde{\bhat}+\bperp)\cdot (\widetilde{\bhat}+\bperp) = |\widetilde{\bhat}|^2 + |\bperp|^2 \end{equation*}
      so that \(\var(\bvec) = \var(\bhat)+\var(\bperp)\text{.}\)
    5. \(|\bvec-A\xhat|^2 = |\bvec-\bhat|^2 = |\bperp|^2\text{,}\) which explains why
      \begin{equation*} \frac{\len{\bvec - A\xhat}^2}{\len{\widetilde{\bvec}}^2} = \frac{\var(\bvec^\perp)}{\var(\bvec)}\text{.} \end{equation*}
      Then
      \begin{equation*} R^2 = 1 - \frac{\len{\bvec - A\xhat}^2}{\len{\widetilde{\bvec}}^2} = 1-\frac{\var(\bvec^\perp)}{\var(\bvec)} = \frac{\var(\bvec) - \var(\bperp)}{\var(\bvec)} = \frac{\var(\bhat)}{\var(\bvec)}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    6. The variances are nonnegative so we have \(R^2 \geq 0\text{.}\) Also,
      \begin{equation*} 1 = \frac{\var(\bvec)}{\var(\bvec)} = \frac{\var(\bhat)+\var(\bperp)}{\var(\bvec)} \geq \frac{\var(\bhat)}{\var(\bvec)}=R^2\text{.} \end{equation*}

    7.1.4.4.

    Answer.
    1. \(B\) is symmetric
    2. \(\displaystyle \vvec\cdot(A^TA\vvec) = (A\vvec)\cdot(A\vvec)\)
    3. \(\displaystyle |A\uvec|^2 = \lambda\)
    4. \(\lambda=|A\uvec|^2 \geq 0\text{.}\)
    5. \(\uvec\cdot(C\uvec) = \frac1N(A^T\uvec)\cdot(A^T\uvec) = \frac1N |A^T\uvec|^2\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. Because \(B^T = (A^TA)^T = A^TA = B\text{,}\) the matrix \(B\) is symmetric and hence orthogonally diagonalizable.
    2. \(\vvec\cdot(B\vvec) = \vvec\cdot(A^TA\vvec) = (A\vvec)\cdot(A\vvec) = |A\vvec|^2\text{.}\)
    3. \(\displaystyle |A\uvec|^2 = \uvec\cdot(B\uvec) = \lambda\uvec\cdot\uvec = \lambda\)
    4. Because \(\lambda=|A\uvec|^2 \geq 0\text{.}\)
    5. In the same way, \(C=\frac1N AA^T\) so that \(\uvec\cdot(C\uvec) = \frac1N(A^T\uvec)\cdot(A^T\uvec) = \frac1N |A^T\uvec|^2\text{.}\) Therefore, \(\lambda = \frac1N|A^T\uvec|^2\text{.}\)

    5. False.
    Solution.
    1. True. If \(A=QDQ^T\) is invertible, then the eigenvalues are nonzero, which means that \(D\) is invertible. Therefore, \(A^{-1} = (Q^T)^{-1}D^{-1}Q^{-1} = QD^{-1}Q^T\text{,}\) which says that \(A^{-1}\) is orthogonally diagonalizable.
    2. True. In this case, there cannot be a basis for \(\real^m\) consisting of eigenvalues of \(A\) so \(A\) is not diagonalizable.
    3. False. This condition implies that \(A\) is diagonalizable, but it may not be orthogonally diagonalizable.
    4. True. The eigenvectors of a symmetric matrix associated to different eigenvalues are orthogonal.
    5. False. This is only true if \(A\) is symmetric.

    7.1.4.9.

    Answer.
    \begin{equation*} D = \begin{bmatrix} 20 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -4 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{bmatrix},\hspace{24pt} Q = \begin{bmatrix} 2/3 \amp 1/\sqrt{18} \amp -1/\sqrt{2} \\ -1/3 \amp 4/\sqrt{18} \amp 0 \\ 2/3 \amp 1/\sqrt{18} \amp 1/\sqrt{2} \\ \end{bmatrix} \end{equation*}
    Solution.
    Since \(A\) is not invertible, the third eigenvalue must be zero: \(\lambda_3=0\text{.}\) Also, an eigenvector associated to \(\lambda_3\) must be orthogonal to both \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\text{.}\) We can find such a vector by finding \(\nul(B^T)\) where \(B=\begin{bmatrix}\vvec_1\amp\vvec_2\end{bmatrix}\text{.}\) This leads to
    \begin{equation*} D = \begin{bmatrix} 20 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -4 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{bmatrix},\hspace{24pt} Q = \begin{bmatrix} 2/3 \amp 1/\sqrt{18} \amp -1/\sqrt{2} \\ -1/3 \amp 4/\sqrt{18} \amp 0 \\ 2/3 \amp 1/\sqrt{18} \amp 1/\sqrt{2} \\ \end{bmatrix} \end{equation*}

    7.1.4.10.

    Answer.
    1. \(P\) is symmetric
    2. 0 or 1
    3. The eigenspaces \(E_1=W\) and \(E_0=W^{\perp}\)
    Solution.
    1. If \(Q\) is a \(3\times2\) matrix whose columns are an orthonormal basis for \(W\text{,}\) then \(P=QQ^T\text{.}\) This means that \(P^T=(Q^T)^TQ^T = QQ^T = P\) so \(P\) is symmetric and hence orthogonally diagonalizable.
    2. If \(\vvec\) is in \(W\text{,}\) then \(P\vvec=\vvec\) and if \(\vvec\) is in \(W^\perp\text{,}\) then \(P\vvec=0\text{.}\) This means that the eigenvalues of \(P\) are either 0 or 1.
    3. The eigenspaces \(E_1=W\) and \(E_0=W^{\perp}\)

    7.2.4.7.

    Answer.
    1. \(q(\uvec) = |B\uvec|^2 = \lambda \geq 0\text{.}\)
    2. \(q_{A+B}(\xvec) = q_A(\xvec) + q_B(\xvec) \gt 0\text{.}\)
    3. The eigenvalues of \(A^{-1}\) are the reciprocals of the eigenvalues of \(A\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. The matrix is symmetric because \((B^TB)^T = B^T(B^T)^T = B^TB\text{.}\) If \(\uvec\) is an eigenvector having unit length, then \(q(\uvec) = \uvec\cdot(B^TB\uvec) = \lambda\) and \(q(\uvec) = \uvec\cdot(B^TB\uvec) = (B\uvec)\cdot(B\uvec) = |B\uvec|^2 = \lambda \geq 0\text{.}\)
    2. If \(\xvec\) is a nonzero vector, then \(q_{A+B}(\xvec) = \xvec\cdot((A+B)\xvec) = \xvec\cdot(A\xvec) + \xvec\cdot(B\xvec) = q_A(\xvec) + q_B(\xvec) \gt 0\text{.}\)
    3. In this case, we know that the eigenvalues of \(A\) are all positive. If \(\lambda\) is an eigenvalue of \(A\text{,}\) then \(1/\lambda\) is an eigenvalue of \(A^{-1}\) so \(A^{-1}\) is also positive definite.

    7.2.4.8.

    Answer.
    1. False
    2. True
    3. True
    4. True
    5. True
    Solution.
    1. False. We know that it is neither positive nor negative definite.
    2. True. This implies that \(q_A(\uvec) \geq 3\) for all unit vectors \(\uvec\text{.}\) Then for any other vector \(q_A(\xvec) =q_A(|\xvec|\uvec)= |\xvec|^2 q_A(\uvec) \gt 0\text{.}\)
    3. True. If \(\lambda\) is an eigenvalue with associated unit eigenvector \(\uvec\text{,}\) then
      \begin{equation*} q_C(\uvec) = \uvec\cdot(C\uvec) = \frac1N\uvec\cdot(AA^T\uvec) = \frac1N(A^T\uvec)\cdot(A^T\uvec) = \frac1N|A^T\uvec|^2 \geq 0\text{.} \end{equation*}
    4. True. The matrix \(Q=\begin{bmatrix} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 1 \\ \end{bmatrix}\) is the identity so \(A = QDQ^T = D\text{.}\)
    5. True. In this case, \(A\) and \(B\) are similar so they have the same eigenvalues, all of which are negative.

    7.2.4.9.

    Answer.
    1. \((\sqrt[3]{2}, \sqrt[3]{2})\) is neither a local maximum nor minimum.
    2. \((0,0)\) is neither a local maximum nor minimum. Both \((1,1)\) and \((-1,-1)\) are local minima.
    Solution.
    1. \begin{align*} f_x \amp {}={} y - \frac2{x^2} = 0\\ f_y \amp {}={} x - \frac2{y^2} = 0 \end{align*}
      Also, \(f_{xx} = \frac4{x^3}\text{,}\) \(f_{xy} = 1\text{,}\) and \(f_{yy} = \frac4{y^3}\) so that the Hessian matrix is \(\begin{bmatrix} 1/2 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 1/2 \end{bmatrix}\text{.}\) The eigenvalues are \(-1/2\) and \(3/2\) so this matrix is indefinite and the critical point is neither a local maximum nor minimum.
    2. \begin{align*} f_x \amp {}={} 4x^3 - 4y = 0\\ f_y \amp {}={} 4y^3 - 4x = 0 \end{align*}
      so \(x=y^3\) and \(y=x^3\text{.}\) Therefore, \(x=x^9\) so \(x=y=0\text{,}\) \(1\text{,}\) or \(-1\text{.}\)
      Also, \(f_{xx} = 12{x^2}\text{,}\) \(f_{xy} = -4\text{,}\) and \(f_{yy} = 12{y^2}\) so that the Hessian matrix is \(\begin{bmatrix} 12x^2 \amp -4 \\ -4 \amp 12y^2 \end{bmatrix}\text{.}\)
      At \((0,0)\text{,}\) the Hessian is indefinite so this critical point is neither a local maximum nor minimum. At both \((1,1)\) and \((-1,-1)\text{,}\) it is positive definite so the critical points are local minima.

    7.2.4.10.

    Answer.
    1. The Hessian matrix at that point is \(H=\begin{bmatrix} 12 \amp 4 \amp -4 \\ 4 \amp 12 \amp 4 \\ -4 \amp 4 \amp 12 \end{bmatrix} \)
    2. \(H\) is positive definite.
    3. The critical point is a local minimum.
    Solution.
    1. \begin{align*} f_x \amp {}={} 4x^3 - 4yz = 0\\ f_y \amp {}={} 4y^3 - 4xz = 0\\ f_z \amp {}={} 4z^3 - 4xy = 0 \end{align*}
      and all three equations are satisfied at the point \((-1,1,-1)\text{.}\)
      The Hessian matrix at that point is \(H=\begin{bmatrix} 12 \amp 4 \amp -4 \\ 4 \amp 12 \amp 4 \\ -4 \amp 4 \amp 12 \end{bmatrix} \)
    2. The eigenvalues are \(4\text{,}\) \(16\text{,}\) and \(16\) so \(H\) is positive definite
    3. This implies that the critical point is a local minimum.

    7.3.4.1.

    Answer.
    1. \(149\) for the first dataset and \(101\) for the second
    2. \(50.3\%\) and \(99.0\%\)
    3. The points in the second set are clustered very close to a line.
    Solution.
    1. For the first dataset, the total variance is \(75+75 = 149\) while the second dataset has a total variance of \(100+1=101\text{.}\)
    2. In the first dataset, the first principal component represents \(75/149=50.3\%\) of the variance compared to \(100/101=99.0\%\) in the second dataset.
    3. The points in the first dataset are scattered almost uniformly around the origin. In the second dataset, they are clustered very close to the line defined by the vector \(\twovec{-1/\sqrt{2}}{1/\sqrt{2}}\text{.}\)

    Solution.
    1. The eigenvalues are \(732\text{,}\) \(114\text{,}\) and \(24\) so the fraction of variance represented by the first two principal components is \((732+114)/(732+114+24) = 97\%\)
    2. Suppose \(\xvec\) is this data point and \(\mvec\) the vector of means. If \(Q\) is the \(2\times3\) matrix whose columns are the first two principal components, then we find \(Q.T(\xvec-\mvec) = \twovec{25.4}{8.16}\text{.}\)
    3. We find \(Q\twovec{12}{-25} + \mvec = \threevec{9.7}{-22.3}{3.7}\text{.}\)

    Answer.
    1. True
    2. False
    3. False
    Solution.
    1. True. The variance of the projected data points is \(V_{\uvec_3} = \uvec_3\cdot(C\uvec_3) = \lambda_3\text{.}\)

    3. False. They will lie on the plane spanned by the first two principal components.

    7.3.4.5.

    Answer.
    1. The petal length is the most signficant and the sepal width is the least.
    2. Iris setosa has a below average petal length and appears overall smaller.
    3. Iris versicolor
    Solution.
    1. The first principal component is \(\uvec_1 = \fourvec{0.36}{-0.08}{0.85}{0.36}\text{.}\) If we move in the \(\uvec_1\) direction, each component describes how we move away from the mean of that measurement. The most important component is the third one, which corresponds to petal length, while the second component, corresponding to sepal width, is least signficant. Of the four measurements, there is the most variance in petal length.
    2. To reach an Iris setosa sample, we multiply \(\uvec_1\) by a negative number. This says that the petal length, the most signficant component of \(\uvec_1\) will be below the mean for Iris setosa. The sepal length and petal width will also be below the mean. Basically, the Iris setosa would appear to be smaller overall.
    3. Iris versicolor would appear to be closest to average since the points are closest to the origin in the \(\uvec_1\)-\(\uvec_2\) plane.

    1. \(\sigma_1=3\text{,}\) \(\vvec_1 = \twovec{1/\sqrt{2}}{1/\sqrt{2}}\)
    2. \(\sigma_2=1\text{,}\) \(\vvec_1 = \twovec{1/\sqrt{2}}{-1/\sqrt{2}}\)
    3. The maximum value of \(q_G(\xvec)\) is \(9\text{,}\) which occurs in the direction \(\twovec{1/\sqrt{2}}{1/\sqrt{2}}\text{.}\) The minimum value of \(q_G(\xvec)\) is \(1\text{,}\) which occurs in the direction \(\twovec{1/\sqrt{2}}{-1/\sqrt{2}}\text{.}\)
    4. \(\sigma_1 = \sqrt{9} = 3\) and \(\sigma_2 = \sqrt{1} = 1\)
    5. \(\vvec_1\) agrees with the direction in which \(q_G(\xvec)\) has its maximum value. The corresponding fact is true for \(\vvec_2\text{.}\)
    6. \(\uvec_1=\twovec{1/\sqrt{2}}{-1/\sqrt{2}}\) and \(\uvec_2 = \twovec{-1/\sqrt{2}}{-1/\sqrt{2}}\)
    7. \(AV = \begin{bmatrix} A\vvec_1 \amp A\vvec_2 \end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix} \sigma_1\uvec_1 \amp \sigma_2\uvec_2 \end{bmatrix} = U\Sigma\text{.}\)
    8. Since \(V\) is an orthogonal matrix, we have \(A = AVV^T = U\Sigma V^T\text{.}\)

    Activity 7.4.3.

    Answer.
    1. \(G\) can be orthogonally diagaonalized with
      \begin{equation*} D = \begin{bmatrix} 3 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 2 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \end{bmatrix},\hspace{24pt} Q = \begin{bmatrix} 1/\sqrt{3} \amp 1/\sqrt{2} \amp 1/\sqrt{6} \\ 1/\sqrt{3} \amp 0 \amp -2/\sqrt{6} \\ 1/\sqrt{3} \amp -1/\sqrt{2} \amp 1/\sqrt{6} \end{bmatrix}. \end{equation*}
    2. \(\sigma_1 = \sqrt{3}\text{,}\) \(\sigma_2 = \sqrt{2}\text{,}\) and \(\sigma_3 = 0\text{.}\) The three right singular vectors \(\vvec_i\) are the columns of \(Q\text{.}\)
    3. \(\uvec_1 = \twovec01\) and \(\uvec_2 = \twovec10\)
    4. We have
      \begin{equation*} U = \begin{bmatrix} 0 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 0 \\ \end{bmatrix},\hspace{24pt} V = \begin{bmatrix} 1/\sqrt{3} \amp 1/\sqrt{2} \amp 1/\sqrt{6} \\ 1/\sqrt{3} \amp 0 \amp -2/\sqrt{6} \\ 1/\sqrt{3} \amp -1/\sqrt{2} \amp 1/\sqrt{6} \end{bmatrix}\text{.} \end{equation*}
    5. With \(\Sigma = \begin{bmatrix} \sqrt{3} \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp \sqrt{2} \amp 0 \end{bmatrix}\text{,}\) we see that \(A=U\Sigma V^T\text{.}\)
    6. \(A^T=(U\Sigma V^T)^T = V\Sigma^T U^T\text{.}\)

    7.4.2 The structure of singular value decompositions

    Activity 7.4.4.

    Answer.
    1. \(A\) is \(4\times3\)
    2. \(c_1 = 2\text{,}\) \(c_2 = 4\text{,}\) and there is no condition on \(c_3\)
    3. \(c_1 = 0\text{,}\) \(c_2 = 0\text{,}\) and there is no condition on \(c_3\)
    4. \(\vvec_3\) forms a basis for \(\nul(A)\text{.}\)
    5. \(\uvec_1\) and \(\uvec_2\) form a basis for \(\col(A)\text{.}\)
    6. \(\rank(A) = 2\text{,}\) which is the number of nonzero singular values.

    7.4.3 Reduced singular value decompositions

    Activity 7.4.5.

    Answer.
    1. \(A\) is a \(4\times3\) matrix and \(\rank(A) = 2\)
    2. \(\uvec_1\) and \(\uvec_2\) form an orthonormal basis for \(\col(A)\text{,}\) and \(\vvec_1\) and \(\vvec_2\) form an orthonormal basis for \(\nul(A)\text{.}\)
    3. Notice that
      \begin{equation*} U\Sigma = \begin{bmatrix} \uvec_1 \amp \uvec_2 \amp \uvec_3 \amp \uvec_4 \end{bmatrix} \begin{bmatrix} 18 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 4 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 0 \amp 0 \end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix} 18\uvec_1 \amp 4\uvec_2 \amp \zerovec \end{bmatrix} \end{equation*}
    4. Notice that
      \begin{equation*} \begin{bmatrix} 18 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 4 \amp 0 \\ \end{bmatrix}V^T = \begin{bmatrix} 18 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 4 \amp 0 \\ \end{bmatrix} \begin{bmatrix} \vvec_1^T \\ \vvec_2^T \\ \vvec_3^T \end{bmatrix} \end{equation*}
    5. Put together the previous parts to see that \(A=U_r\Sigma_rV_r^T\) where
      \begin{equation*} U_r = \begin{bmatrix} \uvec_1 \amp \uvec_2 \end{bmatrix},\hspace{24pt} \Sigma_r = \begin{bmatrix} 18 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp 4 \\ \end{bmatrix},\hspace{24pt} V_r = \begin{bmatrix} \vvec_1 \amp \vvec_2 \end{bmatrix}\text{.} \end{equation*}

      Since \(U=V\text{,}\) this is an orthogonal diagonalization.

In this case, \(G=A^TA = A^2\text{.}\) If \(\lambda_i\geq 0\) are the eigenvalues of \(A\) and \(\vvec_i\) the associated eigenvectors, then \(\lambda_i^2\) is an eigenvalue of \(G\) with associated eigenvector \(\vvec_i\text{.}\) Therefore, \(\sigma_i = \sqrt{\lambda_i^2} = \lambda_i\) and the right singular vectors are \(\vvec_i\text{.}\)
      The left singular vectors are \(A\vvec_i = \sigma_i\uvec_i = \lambda_i\vvec_i\) so the left singular vectors are the same as the right singular vectors.

      \begin{equation*} U=\begin{bmatrix} 1/\sqrt{6} \amp 0 \amp 5/\sqrt{30} \\ 2/\sqrt{6} \amp 1/\sqrt{5} \amp -2/\sqrt{30} \\ 1/\sqrt{6} \amp -2/\sqrt{5} \amp -1/\sqrt{30} \\ \end{bmatrix},\hspace{24pt} \Sigma = \begin{bmatrix} \sqrt{6} \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp \sqrt{5} \\ 0 \amp 0 \\ \end{bmatrix},\hspace{24pt} V = \begin{bmatrix} 1 \amp 0 \\ 0 \amp -1 \\ \end{bmatrix} \end{equation*}
    2. \(\rank(B)=2\text{.}\) A basis for \(\col(B)\) consists of the first two columns of \(U\) and a basis for \(\col(A)^\perp\) consists of the third column of \(U\text{.}\)
    3. If \(Q\) is the matrix consisting of the first two columns of \(U\text{,}\) then \(\bhat = QQ^T\bvec = \threevec226\text{.}\) Then \(\bperp = \bvec - \bhat = \threevec{-5}21\text{.}\)

    7.4.5.7.

    Answer.
    1. \(\displaystyle A^{-1} = V\Sigma^{-1}U^T\)
    2. All the singular values are nonzero.
    3. They are reciprocals of one another.
    4. The left singular vectors of \(A\) are the right singular vectors of \(A^{-1}\) and vice versa.
    Solution.
    1. \(\displaystyle A^{-1} = (U\Sigma^{-1}V^T)^{-1} = V\Sigma^{-1}U^T\)
    2. We need \(\rank(A)=m\) so all the singular values are nonzero. This means that the square matrix \(\Sigma\) is invertible.
    3. If \(\sigma_i\) are the singular values for \(A\text{,}\) then \(1/\sigma_i\) are the singular values for \(A^{-1}\text{.}\)
    4. The left singular vectors of \(A\) are the right singular vectors of \(A^{-1}\) and vice versa.

    7.4.5.8.

    Answer.
    1. \(\displaystyle \det(Q^TQ) = \det(Q^T)\det(Q)=\det(I) \)
    2. \(\displaystyle |\det(A) = |\det(U)|~|\det(\Sigma)|~|\det(V^T)|\)
    3. All the singular values are nonzero.
    Solution.
    1. \(\det(Q^TQ) = \det(Q^T)\det(Q)=(\det(Q))^2 = \det(I) = 1\) so \(\det(Q) = \pm 1\text{.}\)
    2. \(|\det(A) = |\det(U)|~|\det(\Sigma)|~|\det(V^T)| = |\det(\Sigma)|\text{,}\) which equals the product of the singular values.
    3. If \(A\) is invertible, then \(\det(A) \neq 0\text{,}\) which says that all the singular values are nonzero.

    7.4.5.9.

    Answer.
    1. If \(\vvec\) is an eigenvector of \(G\) with eigenvalue \(\lambda\text{,}\) then \(\vvec\cdot(G\vvec) = \lambda\vvec\cdot\vvec\)
    2. If \(A\) is symmetric, then \(G=A^TA = A^2\text{.}\)
    3. If \(\lambda\) is an eigenvalue of \(A\text{,}\) then \(\sigma^2 = \lambda^2\) is an eigenvalue of \(G\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. If \(\vvec\) is an eigenvector of \(G\) with eigenvalue \(\lambda\text{,}\) then \(\vvec\cdot(G\vvec) = \lambda\vvec\cdot\vvec= \lambda|\vvec|^2 = |A\vvec|^2\) so \(\lambda\geq0\text{.}\)
    2. If \(A\) is symmetric, then \(G=A^TA = A^2\text{.}\) Then if \(A\vvec=\lambda \vvec\text{,}\) it follows that \(G\vvec = \lambda^2\vvec\text{.}\)
    3. If \(\lambda\) is an eigenvalue of \(A\text{,}\) then \(\sigma^2 = \lambda^2\) is an eigenvalue of \(G\text{.}\) Then \(\sigma = |\lambda|\text{.}\)

    7.4.5.10.

    Answer.
    1. True
    2. False
    3. False
    4. True
    5. True
    Solution.
    1. True. Remembering that \(U\) is orthogonal, we have \(G = A^TA=(U\Sigma V^T)^T(U\Sigma V^T) = V\Sigma^T U^T U \Sigma V^T = V (\Sigma^T\Sigma)V^T\text{.}\) Since \(\Sigma^T\Sigma\) is diagonal and \(V\) is orthogonal, this is an orthogonal diagonalization.
    2. False. They form a basis for \(\col(A^T)\text{.}\)
    3. False. The singular values are always nonnegative whereas the eigenvalues can take on any sign.
    4. True. This would say that \(\nul(A) = \{\zerovec\}\text{,}\) which implies that the columns are linearly independent.
    5. True. The Gram matrix is always symmetric and hence orthogonally diagonalizable.

    7.4.5.11.

    Answer.
    1. Every column of \(\Sigma\) has a nonzero singular value.
    2. Every row of \(\Sigma\) has a nonzero singular value.
    3. Then \(\Sigma\) is a square matrix and every singular value is nonzero.
    Solution.
    1. If the columns are linearly independent, then \(\nul(A)=\{\zerovec\}\text{,}\) which means that the rank equals the number of columns. Therefore, every column of \(\Sigma\) has a nonzero singular value.
    2. In the columns span \(\real^m\text{,}\) then \(\col(A)=\real^m\text{,}\) which says that the rank equals the number of rows. Therefore, every row of \(\Sigma\) has a nonzero singular value.
    3. Then \(\Sigma\) is a square matrix and every singular value is nonzero.

    7.5.3 Principal component analysis

    Activity 7.5.4.

    Answer.
    1. The fraction of the total variance represented by the first two principal components is \(97.8\%\text{.}\)
    2. If \(\xvec = c_1\uvec_1+c_2\uvec_2+c_3\uvec_3+c_4\uvec_4\text{,}\) then \(\fourvec{c_1}{c_2}{c_3}{c_4}\) is the corresponding column of \(U^TA\text{.}\)
    3. We just need the first two components of the corresponding column of \(\Gamma\text{.}\)
    4. \(\twovec{c_1}{c_2}\) is the corresponding column of \(U_2^TA\text{.}\)
    5. We can construct \(\Gamma_2=\Sigma_2 V_2^T\) or just pull out the first two rows of \(\Gamma=\Sigma V^T\text{.}\)

      \(A_3=\begin{bmatrix} 2.2 \amp -1.9 \amp 0.048 \amp 3.7 \\ -1.2 \amp 2.9 \amp 0.72 \amp -0.72 \\ -0.51 \amp 2.8 \amp -1.4 \amp 4.2 \\ -0.60 \amp 2.3 \amp 2.0 \amp -1.7 \end{bmatrix}\text{,}\) \(A_4=A\)

    7.5.7.2.

    Answer.
    1. The linear system is
      \begin{equation*} \begin{bmatrix} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 2 \amp 4 \\ 1 \amp 3 \amp 9 \\ 1 \amp 4 \amp 16 \\ \end{bmatrix} \xvec = \fivevec10{1.5}48 \end{equation*}
    2. \(U\) is a \(5\times5\) matrix, \(\Sigma\) is \(5\times3\text{,}\) and \(V\) is \(3\times3\)
    3. \(\displaystyle \xhat=\threevec{0.87}{-1.34}{0.79}\)
    Solution.
    1. The linear system is
      \begin{equation*} \begin{bmatrix} 1 \amp 0 \amp 0 \\ 1 \amp 1 \amp 1 \\ 1 \amp 2 \amp 4 \\ 1 \amp 3 \amp 9 \\ 1 \amp 4 \amp 16 \\ \end{bmatrix} \xvec = \fivevec10{1.5}48 \end{equation*}
    2. \(U\) is a \(5\times5\) matrix, \(\Sigma\) is \(5\times3\text{,}\) and \(V\) is \(3\times3\)
    3. The rank of \(A\) is 3 so we’ll form the reduced singular value decomposition \(A=U_3\Sigma_3V_3^T\text{.}\) Then \(\xhat = V_3\Sigma_3^{-1}U_3^T\bvec = \threevec{0.87}{-1.34}{0.79}\)

    7.5.7.3.

    Answer.
    1. \(\displaystyle \uvec~\vvec^T = \begin{bmatrix} 4 \amp 0 \amp 2 \\ -6 \amp 0 \amp -3 \end{bmatrix}\)
    2. The rank is \(1\) and \(\uvec\) forms a basis.
    3. This matrix projects vectors orthogonally onto the line defined by \(\uvec\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    1. \(\displaystyle \uvec~\vvec^T = \begin{bmatrix} 2\uvec \amp 0\uvec \amp \uvec \end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix} 4 \amp 0 \amp 2 \\ -6 \amp 0 \amp -3 \end{bmatrix}\)
    2. Since each column of \(\uvec~\vvec^T\) is a scalar multiple of \(\uvec\text{,}\) the rank of the matrix is \(1\) and \(\uvec\) forms a basis.
    3. This matrix projects vectors orthogonally onto the line defined by \(\uvec\text{.}\)

    7.5.7.4.

    Answer.
    1. The variances are \(1.96\text{,}\) \(1.01\text{,}\) \(0.79\text{,}\) and \(0.24\text{.}\)
    2. \(\displaystyle 74\%\)
    3. The matrix \(B\) is formed from the first two rows of the product \(\Sigma V^T\text{.}\)
    4. Left
    5. Houses on the left have larger living area, lot sizes, and prices and are newer. Houses near the top have larger lot sizes and ages.
    Solution.
    1. The singular values \(\sigma_i\) are \(45.5\text{,}\) \(32.6\text{,}\) \(28.9\text{,}\) and \(15.9\text{.}\) Therefore, the variances in the direction of the principal components are given by \(\frac{1}{1057}\sigma_i^2\) and are \(1.96\text{,}\) \(1.01\text{,}\) \(0.79\text{,}\) and \(0.24\text{.}\)
    2. The first two principal components retain \(74\%\) of the total variance.
    3. The matrix \(B\) is formed from the first two rows of the product \(\Sigma V^T\text{.}\)
    4. The fourth components, the ones that correspond to house prices, of \(\uvec_1\) and \(\uvec_2\) are \(-0.65\) and \(-0.03\) respectively. This shows us that \(\uvec_2\) doesn’t contribute much to the house price. Moving in the negative \(\uvec_1\) direction causes the house price to go up so the more expensive homes are on the left.
    5. The first principal component is \(\uvec_1 = \fourvec{-0.64}{-0.20}{0.36}{-0.65}\text{.}\) If we move to the left, this vector is multiplied by a negative number so the living area, lot size, and price go up while the age of the house decreases.
      The second principal component is \(\uvec_2=\fourvec{0.05}{0.86}{0.51}{-0.03}\text{.}\) If we move up, this is multiplied by a positive number so the lot size and the age of the house increase.

    7.5.7.5.

    Answer.
    1. For each justice, it tells us the number of times that justice voted with the majority minus the number of times they voted in the minority.
    2. The justice that most often votes with the majority could be a swing vote.
    3. The first five justices usually vote in the majority.
    4. A second justice voted with the majority 84 times more than with the minority.
    5. Anthony Kennedy
    Solution.
    1. \(A\vvec\) is a 9-dimensional vector. For each justice, it tells us the number of times that justice voted with the majority minus the number of times they voted in the minority.
    2. Out of 188 cases, the fifth justice Sandra Day O’Connor voted with the majority 94 more times than with the minority. Since a swing vote will often vote in the majority, this gives additional evidence that Sandra Day O’Connor is a swing vote.
    3. The first five justices more often vote with the majority and the last four more often with the minority. This means the court leans toward the first five justices, who are the more conservative ones.
    4. The fourth justice Anthony Kennedy voted with the majority 84 times more than with the minority. He is a potential swing vote.
    5. The third singular vector \(\uvec_3\) effectively records his vote and not the votes of the other justices. This lends support to the claim that Anthony Kennedy is a second, somewhat less influential, swing vote.

    7.5.7.6.

    Answer.
    1. The matrix is symmetric.
    2. \(A_2\) should be a good approximation to \(A\text{.}\)
    3. \(\displaystyle U=V\)
    4. John Paul Stevens, is the least agreeable.
    5. The fourth and fifth justices, Anthony Kennedy and Sandra Day O’Connor are less inclined to agree with the others.
    Solution.
    1. The matrix is symmetric because the number of times Justice A votes with Justice B is the same as the number of times Justice B votes with Justice A.
    2. The first two singular values appear to be most important so \(A_2\) should be a good approximation to \(A\text{.}\)
    3. \(U=V\) since \(A\) is a positive definite, symmetric matrix.
    4. The sixth justice, John Paul Stevens, is the least agreeable.
    5. The fourth and fifth justices, Anthony Kennedy and Sandra Day O’Connor are less inclined to agree with the others so they form swing votes.

    7.5.7.7.

    Answer.
    1. Find the number of columns of \(U_r^T\) and the number of rows of \(V_r\text{.}\)
    2. If \(A\) is invertible, then \(A\) is an \(m\times m\) matrix whose rank is \(m\text{.}\)
    3. \(\displaystyle AA^{+} = (U_r\Sigma_rV_r^T)(V_r\Sigma^{-1}U_r^T) = U_rU_r^T\)
    4. \(\displaystyle AA^{+} = (V_r\Sigma^{-1}U_r^T)(U_r\Sigma_rV_r^T) V_rV_r^T\)
    Solution.
    1. The number of columns of \(A^+\) is the number of columns of \(U_r^T\text{,}\) which is \(m\text{,}\) and the number of rows is the number of rows of \(V_r\text{,}\) which is \(n\text{.}\)
    2. If \(A\) is invertible, then \(A\) is an \(m\times m\) matrix whose rank is \(m\text{.}\) The reduced singular value decomposition is therefore \(A=U\Sigma V^T\) so that \(A^+=V\Sigma^{-1}U^T = (U\Sigma V^T)^{-1}=A^{-1}\text{.}\)
    3. \(AA^{+} = (U_r\Sigma_rV_r^T)(V_r\Sigma^{-1}U_r^T) = U_rU_r^T\text{,}\) which projects vectors orthogonal onto \(\col(A)\) since the columns of \(U_r\) are an orthonormal basis for \(\col(A)\text{.}\)
    4. \(AA^{+} = (V_r\Sigma^{-1}U_r^T)(U_r\Sigma_rV_r^T) V_rV_r^T\text{,}\) which projects vectors orthogonal onto \(\col(A^T)\) since the columns of \(V_r\) are an orthonormal basis for \(\col(A^T)\text{.}\)

    7.5.7.8.

    Answer.
    1. The condition number is \(40,000\)
      1. \(\displaystyle A^{-1}\bvec = \twovec{-14142.5}{14142.5}\)
      2. \(\displaystyle A^{-1}\bvec = \twovec{-14142.8}{14142.8}\)
      1. \(\displaystyle A^{-1}\bvec = \twovec{-14142.5}{14142.5}\)
      2. \(\displaystyle A^{-1}\bvec = \twovec{-28284.6}{28285.3}\)
    4. \(\displaystyle c_2=\frac{d_2}{\sigma_2}\)
    Solution.
    1. The singular values of \(A\) are \(2\) and \(5\times10^{-5}\text{,}\) which means the condition number is \(40,000\text{.}\)
      1. \(\displaystyle A^{-1}\bvec = \twovec{-14142.5}{14142.5}\)
      2. \(\displaystyle A^{-1}\bvec = \twovec{-14142.8}{14142.8}\)
      1. \(\displaystyle A^{-1}\bvec = \twovec{-14142.5}{14142.5}\)
      2. \(\displaystyle A^{-1}\bvec = \twovec{-28284.6}{28285.3}\)
    4. We have \(d_i = \sigma_ic_i\) so \(c_i=\frac{d_i}{\sigma_i}\text{.}\) Since \(\sigma_2\) is so much smaller than \(\sigma_1\text{,}\) even small changes in \(d_2\) can lead to relatively large changes in \(c_2\text{.}\)
    And a conclusion to this solutions division, which may not be readily apparent as distinct from the final division’s worth of solutions, but since it is not prefixed with a number, it may be different enough.
